Author’s Note:

This is a work of fiction.  While a number of the incidents in this book are inspired by somewhat similar ones that occurred in the author’s life, the ones described here are quite different, in that in each case the author has sought to portray his alter ego as far more wise, brave, capable, handsome, and sexy than the author is in real life, and the other people as far more petty, ill-tempered, shallow, stupid, and vain than they really are.  He has also forgotten most of the relevant details and made up others just to please himself and show himself in a better light.  Many other incidents have been completely fabricated, primarily to boost the author’s ego and also to provide some pretense at a narrative arc, where in real life events just bumble along randomly.

If you are acquainted with the author and suspect that a character in these pages is based on you, and you are not happy about the characterization, please remember that the author’s poor writing skills and shallow, narcissistic soul mean that he is capable of using his words only to create crude, unsympathetic caricatures of people. [a] The character on these pages is not you, just possibly a half-baked parody awkwardly sketched to entertain, doubtless including details borrowed from entirely different people, with no pretense of verisimilitude.  If, on the other hand, you appreciate a characterization that you suspect is based on you, the author wants you to know that this is precisely how he sees you as a person, and he would be pleased to accept gifts and/or sexual favors in recompense for sharing that rendition of you.  (Especially sexual favors, though if you actually read the book you would doubtless be able to guess this.)

Climate Change.

Chapter 3: Here.  It actually began here.

Art.  

As I was trying to reinvigorate my marriage, I was aware of a budding interest in art, and in particular of representational art celebrating the human figure.   Not to put too fine a point on it, in paintings of naked people.  Mostly paintings of naked women.

I decided I needed to get better at flirting, to get more practice.  To try out flirting with different people, to see how it felt, to be more relaxed.  To try it in places where I wouldn’t have to shift an existing pattern.  So I would be better and more relaxed and in the future better able to flirt with Mary.

I flirted a little with some of my female fellow artists, but it was awkward and I decided not to risk more.   I flirted a little with models, maladroitly, garnering a reputation as a creep.  I tried flirting with wait staff at restaurants, but I never seemed to figure out what to say until I was on the way home.

I was doing my day job, running technology standardization meetings for a financial industry association, when one day a new member, Ekaterina, joined.  She was an anomaly - she worked for the government, unlike most members, she was a woman, unlike most members,, and she was not afraid to speak out, also unlike most members..   And she was very, very funny.   I very much enjoyed her presence at my meetings, noticing how much she enlivened them.

One day Ekaterina wrote me an email, looking for a “a good man” for some position  … she was trying to recruit for some committee.  I took this as an opening for some gentle flirtation, suggesting that while I might be a good man, I was not an entirely good man, and I might not be just the man for that position.  But perhaps there were other positions I would enjoy with her…  She clearly enjoyed this missive and its flirtatious insinuation, and to my joy and surprise, she didn’t huff at my inappropriate note, but rather returned her own flirtatious response.

And we were off to the races … the flirtation escalated rapidly, veering into romantic greetings in Italian, (“Ciao, bella!”), intense sexual innuendo, and so on…  It was a new and heady experience for me, to have such a sexy conversation with a woman, and a woman I hadn’t actually met.  It felt exciting and dangerous and fun.

This went on for some weeks, then as we lived far apart and there was little chance to meet, our online flirtation eventually died down, and we stopped our near daily messages.

Then in late 2011, as my art infatuation started to take off, I got notice that I had a meeting in the city that was, Ekaterina’s home base.    I reached out again, letting her know that I would be nearby, and would she like to meet?

Thus in mid December 2011, after a boring meeting with some government officials, I found myself waiting downstairs at a drab office building, planning to meet Ekaterina for lunch.   She came downstairs in a flurry, a tiny, cute ball of energy, and rapidly in Russian-accented English drew me into a nearby sandwich shop for a quick bite. We talked of nothing - she told me, “Russians turn sadness into black humor”, we talked of the craziness of government bureaucracy, of mathematics, of my drawing and how I’d love to draw her someday (she laughed at that), of my unhappy marriage, and vaguely of her recent divorce - and before I knew it, she was standing to leave and dragging me back to her office entrance.  Then off she ran in a flurry to her next meeting.  It had been about 45 minutes since she had pranced downstairs.

I slowly walked to my next engagement - I’d planned to spend part of the afternoon at a small private gallery with some lovely sculptures, and some wonderful large portraits by Sargent.   I pondered this conversation, this sprightly, funny, sad, and heartfelt conversation, with a cute and smart woman 10 years or more my junior.  This amazing and wonderful and intelligent and joyful conversation, that had made me laugh so much.

As I walked down the street, I noticed a tightness in my chest.  This tightness increased rapidly, into an ache, then into a sharp, shooting pain.   I panicked, thinking “Heart attack!” Or maybe it was an acute case of angina?  I wondered whether my Canadian health insurance would cover me if I needed to go to hospital.   I sat down on a nearby park bench, looking for my phone, trying to decide whether to call 911.  I breathed heavily, and tried to catch my breath and collect my wits, and to see if that intense pain would continue to get worse, frantic with worry.

But after a couple of minutes had passed, I noticed something strange:  This wasn’t an ordinary pain, some kind of angina, or your run of the mill heart attack.  There wasn’t pain down the left arm, or in the armpit area.  It was solidly in the middle of my chest, tightly squeezing the heart.   My heart seemed to be beating at close to a normal rhythm. I didn’t seem faint.  My heart was in a vise, but beating normally.

This pain, I slowly concluded,  was something else:  This was heartache, the intense, sharp heartache of love at first sight.  This was Cupid’s arrow, lodged firmly and painfully in my ticker.  Thinking back, I could almost hear the thrum of the arrow vibrating as it struck me, the arrowhead wedged in my chest.  This was love, intense love, something I’d never felt in that particular way before.  Something I’d read about, but never believed really existed.   Something that poets conjure up, something that Medieval knights suffered, but that never really happened to mere mortals.  But maybe it did, because it seems that it had happened to me.

I sat on the bench, gobsmacked by this new feeling, unsure what to do next.   After a time, as the agony faded back to a strong ache,  I drifted in a daze to the gallery, and slowly moved through its halls, eyes barely looking at the beautiful statues and lush paintings, set in a lavish mansion with stone floors and marble stairs.  Eventually I sat in front of a large full length portrait of a woman in a white dress by Sargent, and considered my life.

Could I leave Mary and be with Ekaterina?  Would Ekaterina take me?  What should I do next?

After several hours of contemplation of the esteemed lady on the canvas and of my fate, I went to the airport to catch my flight home, as there seemed to be nothing else to do.   As I waited for my plane, drinking Bloody Marys and worrying, a terrible thought struck me:   I had been fixed - I’d had a vasectomy.  And Ekaterina would want to have children, nay, I would want to have children with her.  And there was no guarantee that my vasectomy could be reversed.  And maybe she wouldn’t want me because of that.  And why, oh, why had I got that vasectomy, and why did I think I would never want to have kids again?  Grief and regret at the poor decisions I’d made in the past suffused my heart; I was suddenly sick with desire to marry Ekaterina and to have children with her …

I came back to Toronto, in a crazy daze, and all I could think of was Ekaterina.   I wrote to her the next day.  “I’m coming back to see you, Ekaterina.  I’m head over heels in love! I’m going to camp on your doorstep until you take me in.   I’m leaving my wife and kids and making a new life with you.  Where do you live?”

She laughed back in her email reply.  “This  is all very cute,” she said, “but I’m moving to Belgium tomorrow, and so your trip will be in vain!”.   Naturally, I didn’t believe her, assuming it was an evasion, but it was true:  she moved to Belgium that week to work at an international financial agency, and though I tried to convince her to meet with me on my various trips to Europe, she managed to slip through my fingers.  And while we remained in touch for years, that was the only time I met her.

So my infatuation with Ekaterina came to nothing, although it took three months or more to die down, and lead to some wonderful masturbatory orgasms…  But it did accomplish something:  It unlocked my heart, and led to my awareness that I didn’t have all of what I wanted in my marriage, that I was missing something that I had never felt was that important:  Romantic love.  

I noticed that by constantly making an effort, I could get Mary to warm up to me a little, but the moment I stopped, she dropped back to ignoring me, treating me as an inconvenient nuisance.   I noticed that it seemed that every woman in the world was more into me than Mary was.   And I didn’t like it. And I felt that I deserved more.

From then on I started looking at my life for love and connection (and sex and appreciation and adoration and so many other activities and feelings).  I noticed that this had never been a priority for me, and grieved that it hadn’t been.   I decided that the Ekaterina infatuation was more than a surprising brief brain tempest, but rather was a deep welling up from my subconscious of an extraordinarily important need, one that I had suppressed for a very long time:  the need to feel connected, and to feel admired and appreciated and cared for by someone close to me in a way that I hadn’t felt in years. Instead of being endured as a miserable source of income who was needy and unpleasant, to be at best humored and avoided.  In short, to feel romantic love for someone, and to feel loved in return:  to feel important and necessary to the people closest to me.

Now this was truly 45 minutes to change my life!

***

Amber was snoring contentedly, sprawled across the ottoman, her legs akimbo, pussy stretched fetchingly.  Jack stared into the embers of the fire, sighing, “Ahh, love.”  Cassandra had headed to bed an hour before - she’d heard all of this before.  And Monica and Dan had headed out to the hot tub for another spin before bed.

Jack said, “In the morning, tell me about your childhood.  Did you have a lot of friends?  Did you do well in school?  Did you date girls?  How is it that love was never important to you?”

I yawned and promised to do that, and finished sipping my dark rum and Amaretto cocktail, banked the fire, slid into bed with Cassandra, fondling her gorgeous body to sleepy appreciative murmurs, and drifted into a contented sleep.

Chapter 5:  The Nerd

Over coffee the next morning Jack guided me back to my early years.  “Do I understand,” he asked, “that when you were young you were more focused on academics and achievement than on people and relationships?”

I sighed, and stared into my coffee.

Eventually, I started.  “Let me tell you,” I said to Jack, “about how school was for me.”

***

I’m doing my grade 4 math homework, which I normally blaze through without a pause, and am puzzling over what appears to be a simple question.  “A clock is set for 1:30 pm.   How many times does the minute hand pass the hour hand when the time goes to 2:30 pm?  From 1:30 until 3:30 pm?  From 1:30 until 7:30 pm?”   This seems so easy - the minute hand passes by the hour hand once each hour, so the answers should be 1 and 2 and 6.   But something doesn’t seem quite right about the final question… Does the minute hand actually pass by the hour hand six times?   Studying a clock and then playing with a watch soon convinces me otherwise - the hour hand’s slow motion past the half hour mark means that the minute hand never actually catches up to the hour hand during one rotation past the bottom of the clock, and so loses a lap around 6:30.  So the correct answer to the last question must be only 5.   This surprises me, because it strikes me as a more difficult than usual question for 4th grade, but I’m proud that I’ve noticed the trick and evaded it.

A few days later when my graded homework assignment is distributed in class, I see that my answer of 5 has been graded incorrect.  A hot streak of shame jolts my body.  Had I really got this wrong?   I check my work, confirming my analysis, then complain to the teacher that this must be in error.   She has none of it.  “See, here’s the answer in the teacher’s manual,” she says - “It’s 6.  You are wrong.”

An intense jolt of energy hits me, and my teeth grind together.  I had tested my theory several times.  I'm positive that I’m right.  How could the official answer be wrong?   I leap up in frustration and run to the demonstration clock at the back of the room, with hands cut out from construction paper and used to help students learn to tell time.   “See,” I shout, “let me show you!!”  I twirl the ersatz clock’s hands and count the crossings. “See, the hands pass only 5 times!”   Noticing the teacher’s slack expression, I repeat the demonstration, sensing that the possibly dim-witted teacher hadn’t absorbed the lesson the first time. The other students stare blankly..

“Sit down, Bobby,” she sighs.

I try again:  “Look! Can’t you see that the book is wrong!”  

Exasperated, she snaps, “Quiet!  To your seat!”

 “Fuck you!” I yell in frustration.   The class gasps and murmurs in shock.   Bobby swore at the teacher!

After a long wait in the principal’s waiting room, my young stomach churning,  I’m ushered inside.  The principal offers me a peppermint and asks, “Now, what’s this all about?”

I stammer out an explanation, waving my arms to indicate the hands of the clock.

He makes a face like he’s eaten a slightly moldy berry in an otherwise delightful bowl.  “Look, Bobby, I understand that it is upsetting to be wrong, but we need to be polite to our teachers in class.”

I protest.  His hand goes up, indicating silence.  My words sputter to a stop.  I sit, uncomfortably. “Wait here,” he says, indicating a chair in the waiting room.

Hours later, or maybe days, or maybe even years, my Mom shows up.  After conferring with the principal, she comes out to see me.  She listens vaguely to my story, and says, “Dear, dear … but you mustn’t be rude to the teacher!”  

I recounted this story many times over the years to portray my passion for the truth, against all odds.  And how I never really trusted authority figures after that. I didn’t talk about how lonely I’d felt, and how painful it was to feel let down by my teacher and principal and mother.

There were times that having a big brain made me sad and lonely.  And this was one.

***

After this incident, and some others of a similar ilk, there were whispers about how Bobby Lindo was “hyper” and got bored easily and was difficult.  I was asked to visit a school psychologist.   Apparently the psychologist discovered nothing catastrophic, possibly a case of ADD mixed with some giftedness, because soon my mom was suggesting that I might apply for a scholarship to Quebec Preparatory School, a prestigious private school in Montreal, a scholarship targeted to smart and less-advantaged boys.   Maybe I would be less bored there, she mused.

I took the scholarship exams along with some dozens of my peers.  It must have gone well, because soon I was invited back for second round interviews, along with a handful of other boys.  I seemed to answer most of the questions to the satisfaction of the interviewers.   The chair of the alumni association and the headmaster were curious about my knowledge of politics and current events - they wanted me to name the leader of the federal official opposition party, the Conservatives.   I was stymied - the name didn’t come to mind.  But I did manage the names of the leaders of the other opposition parties, the Social Credits and the New Democratic Party.   For years afterwards, my mother bought me “Stanfield’s” underwear for Christmas to remind me of when I forgot about the bland and unmemorable Bob Stanfield.[b]

In any event despite this lapse the interviewers must have decided that I fit the bill, because I was awarded a scholarship to QPS and soon found myself traveling for an hour each way every day from my hometown of Dorval, in the West Island suburbs of Montreal, into the Van Horne area of Montreal.  I would get a ride in a smoke-filled car with a classmate and his father, and ride or take the bus home.

The discipline and intellectual pace of QPS mostly agreed with me.  I enjoyed learning, and put up with how hard I needed to work on my homework assignments to finish them all.  These assignments got more and more difficult over the years … in particular I remember busting my brains on geometry proofs.   I was the top student or close every year, and excelled in Physics and Math in particular.[c]

In tenth and eleventh grades I entered various math contests - the Junior Math Contest, the Euclid, and several others.   I had a series of strong results - Quebec Provincial champion, top 10 in Canada, and so on.  I was invited to a week-long math camp at University of Waterloo, where I learned a little number theory.  (I still remember learning about the Riemann Zeta function, and thinking how very odd and useless it was, but discovering how much weird math there was out there that one could learn, should one be interested).   And I attended another several day long[d] math camp at Carleton University

As grade 11 started, my guidance counselor started asking me about my plans for the next year.   I hadn’t thought about it, assuming that I would head on to one of the CEGEPs (junior colleges) in Quebec.   He asked me if I had considered going to university in the US.    The question was startling - but then I realized that I had always admired MIT, because I wanted to follow in my father’s footsteps and be an engineer.  So I agreed to go to a US college recruitment meeting at the school to learn more.

I found the university pitches rather interesting.  Colgate made a pitch that focused on their (apparently amazing) male choir, the Colgate 13.  This baffled me.  What did singing have to do with going to university?   Several of the schools talked about how nice looking their campuses were.  This was also confusing.  Shouldn’t they be talking about their education?

The other exciting feature of the college recruitment meeting was that they had an open bar.  I felt very grown up when I sidled up to the bar and ordered a gin & tonic from the bartender.   The first sip convinced me that I’d made an enormous mistake - its eye-watering bitterness triggered my gag reflex..  I doubted I’d be able to finish.  But I kept at it, and by the end of the second G&T that evening  I’d acquired a lifelong taste for them.

 I did a series of standardized tests - SATs, AP tests, achievement tests, etc. - and my scores were strong enough enough that my guidance counselor convinced me that I should go for it. 

I did a series of campus visits, seeing Princeton, Yale, Harvard, MIT, and Rensselaer Polytechnic Institute, near Albany (RPI).  My top choice going in was still MIT, but coming out of the tours I had to admit that I liked how beautiful the campuses at Princeton and Harvard were, and how interesting and engaged the students were, especially at Harvard.  I even got to stay overnight with a Harvard undergraduate - and I was surprised by their priorities.  The most important things on their minds seemed to be entertainment options: what movies would they see that weekend, where should they go to eat dinner, who was dating whom.   They did mention in passing that they had classes, it didn’t seem to be a focus.  This was an academically prestigious school!?   It was discomfiting.   I noticed how beautiful the Harvard dorms were, and how quaint the ivy-covered buildings looked, and how lively the student body appeared to be.  Particularly the female students, a welcome sight after years of boys-only at QPS.

MIT, on the other hand, boasted an enormously long, dark, echoey corridor that connected most of the buildings, and a lot of pallid, pimply male nerds who spoke in a whine, and blackboards covered with equations.  Their student tour guide boasted about how the engineering students would each year disassemble  a VW Beetle and reassemble it somewhere inconvenient, like in the dean’s office, or up in the cupola of a bell tower.  Nerdy good fun perhaps, but I wasn’t sure that fun would be enough to carry me through a full four years.

When my acceptances came in, I’d been accepted to Harvard, MIT, and RPI, and waitlisted at Princeton (Yale hadn’t made the cut).  It came down to a choice of Harvard and MIT - RPI was the backup plan, and the small town and 8:1 male to female ratio didn’t appeal, despite its beautiful data center built into a remodeled old church, and lit by stained glass windows..

Harvard vs. MIT  was a surprisingly difficult choice - MIT was the place that I’d dreamed of and admired for years, but Harvard was so much more beautiful, engaging, and varied.  It just looked like a lot more fun, truth be told.  It wasn’t as good for engineering, my planned degree, but I reasoned that I could somehow make it work…. I could go to MIT for a graduate engineering degree, if I wanted.

After a week or so of soul searching, I opted for Harvard.

***

Harvard was a revelation.   Everything seemed dripping with tradition.  The more socially sophisticated students seemed to have figured everything else, where I was somewhat lost at first.

There was a terrific flurry of activity the first few weeks:  finding one’s dorm room, moving in, going through an elaborate pre-registration process where we picked up material (among it our course catalog and class signup sheet) and ran through a gauntlet of upperclassmen trying to persuade us naive frosh to join their student organizations, like the Harvard Crimson newspaper or the Harvard Lampoon or the Hasty Pudding theatrical club.  Then course selection, starting with a “shopping period” where the students audited a variety of lectures to see which ones seemed most appealing.

I quickly came to understand how special it was to be  at Harvard: rushing to 9 am Physics class each morning, late, I would have to wend around and through the large hordes of Japanese tourists taking selfies in front of John Harvard’s statue.   These people actually wanted to be up at that ridiculous time!  To see this ridiculous statue! And they were in my way, dammit!

I was quickly immersed into a much bigger student body than I was used to.  My high school had 70 boys or so each year.  Harvard had 1600 incoming freshmen.  And they all seemed so smart, talented, accomplished, and good looking.

I roomed down the hall from a Juilliard-trained cellist.  I took Physics classes with a classmate who had chained himself to the gates of the Seabrook nuclear power plant, organizing a state-wide protest.  Living upstairs in my dorm was the daughter of a world-famous photographer. My friends and roommates had studied Greek and Latin at prep school, or knew about exotic musical forms.  Even the football jocks, who talked in crude Brooklynese and dragged their knuckles on the ground, successfully executed a complex “Multiflex” offense, baffling their opponents, and talked of Heidegger and Diderot in line at the cafeteria.  And everyone dressed preppy, a look I could recognize and admire but couldn’t imagine how to inhabit.

For the first time in years in school, I didn’t stick out.  I wasn’t the smartest in my class. No one particularly noticed me.  I was part of the crowd. No one bullied me or teased me for being too smart. My peers knew all kinds of things that I didn’t, like how to read ancient Greek, or what Martin Heidegger’s philosophy meant, or which indie rock bands were emerging from Athens, Georgia.  

I loved learning from my few friends, and being challenged.   I loved the variety of types of stimulation - the variety of old movies that were screened each weekend (I developed a love for Katherine Hepburn and Cary Grant, starting with The Philadelphia Story), the student plays that were produced, the publications, and so on.

Second semester freshman year, early February of 1982,  I started auditing a class in the philosophy of government, taught by a screechy German philosopher named Judith Shklar.   It was a two-hour lecture directly after a carb-rich lunch, and I would need to fight to stay awake.  My eyelids would flutter shut as Dr. Shklar droned about classical and medieval philosophy, tossing in Greek ideas like agape and kudos.

But as I struggled to take in this monotone, I realized that there were nuggets of gold in her words.

First we read Plato, and learned all about his amazing, wonderful, thoughtful ways of understanding government and politics.  It all seemed so wise and true, and Prof. Shklar’s insights were thought-provoking..  Then we read Aristotle, and his insightful categorization and listings of the various ways that city states would govern themselves.  And his words also seemed so wise and thoughtful and true.   And then I noticed, to my surprise and chagrin, that Plato and Aristotle had totally different ways to see the world, completely different ways to understand things.  And that they often contradicted each other.

How could this be?  How could these two revered, brilliant philosophers, so thoughtful, so wise, so right, how could they disagree?  And which of them was right?  It didn’t seem obvious, though I had a leaning for Plato’s idealism.

This was my introduction to critical thinking - to noticing that just because someone is smart and makes a good argument, it doesn’t mean that they are right.  That we need to figure out our own values for ourselves, and test the strength of other thinkers’ arguments.  Shklar’s lectures pointed out some of the similarities and contrasts between the different philosophers - men like Thomas Aquinas and John of Salisbury and Thomas Hobbes - and helped us see that no one has a monopoly on truth, that getting closer to it requires thought and discernment.

After a week or so of this, I decided that this kind of mind-opening, inspiring learning was what I really wanted to focus on in university.  About how to think critically about all kinds of things, not just philosophy, but history, literature, and anything else that caught my fancy.  So I signed up for a couple of inspirational classes along with my challenging but narrower math and physics and engineering classes.  Along with Shklar’s political philosophy I took a fun developmental psychology class that included a strong dollop of ethnology.

As the semester got going, I realized that the workload of this was going to be heavy.  And that as an engineering student, this would be nearly my last chance to study much of the humanities.  And that in the following years I would be doing many, many engineering classes, and relatively few non-science classes.  And that, to be frank, I wasn’t enjoying my engineering very much.

I went for a long walk, studying the Charles River at the South end of campus.  As I crossed and recrossed it, leaning over the parapets of the bridges, watching the cold Charles eddying by,  I asked myself:  how much does this vision of being an engineer still draw me?  How much do I want to learn about new things, about different things, about the humanities, and social sciences?   Could I give up Engineering?

By the end of my walk I had convinced myself to drop my Engineering class and change my concentration to Physics, which (as a bachelor of arts instead of a bachelor of sciences) had a much lower number of required science classes, and therefore would allow me to take many more humanities and social sciences classes.

This felt like a bit of a cheat and a cop-out, but was also supremely exciting.   I could take whatever classes that I wanted!

For the next three years I turned course selection into an art form, taking classes in history and historiography, classics, cognitive psychology, literature, biology, music, cinema, art, and art history, along with more technical classes like economics and computer science..  I chose my courses largely based on how imaginative and engaging the professor was.  (I dropped “Comedy and the Novel” because I found the first lecture stultifying, for instance.  The surgery was successful but the patient died.)  

I found exhilarating inspiration in all the different ways to think that I was exposed to, the myriad mental and analytical frameworks.  I started noticing patterns across courses, finding similarities in themes in different domains, and using that to develop a personal theory of learning and understanding.  I had learned how to learn, in a way that suited me well, and let me rapidly assimilate new areas – a skill that proved useful the rest of my life.

***

Late Junior (third) year I started developing an interest in chaos theory, a then-new and somewhat arcane branch of physics and math.   I decided that I would like to do an undergraduate thesis in nonlinear dynamics.

I needed to find an advisor.  This proved more difficult than expected.  I spoke with a number of professors in the Math and Physics department. I was intrigued by Shlomo Sternberg’s trademark black beret, much more stylish than the yarmulkes that some other professors wore.  But none of these brilliant, revered figures had time for a lowly Physics undergrad who was interested in this oddball branch of math.  Eventually someone pointed me towards the Applied Physics department, where a lowly condensed matter physicist was playing around with chaos in semiconductors.   It wasn’t much, but it was all I could get, so I took it.

As my first task, Professor Oostenheim assigned me to present a review article covering various types of chaos to a physics colloquium. The review article was long, perhaps 50 pages of closely typeset text with formulas and diagrams, summarizing the result of a large number of scientific papers, covering different types of chaos. It was essentially a long encyclopedia article aimed at scientists and laying out the current state of chaos theory.

Starting with the first discovered Lorenz attractor (the “butterfly effect”), it delved into different types and aspects of chaos, many of them new to me, such as period bifurcation into chaos as described by Mitch Feigenbaum, iterated 1-D chaotic maps, Pomeau-Manneville intermittency, Van der Pol oscillations, and so on.   It was a pretty tall order for me to get familiar with all this new and complex material  in a week or two, and to be ready to present this to several dozen professors, post-docs, and graduate students in a Friday afternoon session.

With considerable effort, I managed to get a good intuitive sense of each of the types of chaos.   At the colloquium I was gamely presenting the material with metaphorical explanations and diagrams, when the questions started to come from Prof. Oostenheim.  They were detailed, incisive, challenging:  Which previous study was this work based on? What did this parameter mean,  exactly?  Could I write down the formula describing that type of chaos?  I noticed my throat closing, my midriff clenching, and my face flushing.

Rarely had I been so unprepared for the piercing, precise questions of a teacher, rarely had I been so caught out of my depth.  My lack of knowledge and the shallowness of my familiarity with the topic was humiliatingly on display in front of a room full of Harvard physicists.   I found myself mumbling, “I don’t know” and “I don’t remember” into the silence, again and again; no one rescued me.  I sweated and mumbled and apologized and proceeded haltingly.  It felt so unfair and so unnecessary; there was a tightness in my gut, and my eyes were burning with shame.

 I managed to hold back the tears brimming my eyes, but it was a near run thing.   I felt blindsided and angry, like I had been asked to perform an impossible task, set up to fail.  Afterwards, I miserably stood around at the post-presentation wine and cheese, trying to avoid catching anyone’s eye.  A sympathetic grad student eventually hove to, commented “Wow, that was a tough one!” with a wry smile, then sauntered off.  The post docs chatted and laughed together, occasionally glancing over towards me, shaking their heads.

I concluded  that Oostenheim had decided to haze me, to try to scare me off.  But I refused to go.

After that ignominious display, Prof. Oostenheim assigned me to work with a postdoc, washing his hands of me. Although he did allow me to use resources in his lab.  I wouldn’t speak to him again until nearly the end of the year, many months later.

In the meanwhile, I started studying a nonlinear dynamical system that Oostenheim and the student had identified, a system that sometimes exhibited chaotic behavior.  The dynamical system was represented by a system of three nonlinear equations.  Could I figure out when it would exhibit chaos?  What values of the parameters of the equation would bring on the non-linear effects?

I built a computer program to simulate the dynamical system, and started working late nights running simulations and trying to understand how it worked.  As I ran the program and it generated results, I found myself intensely curious, trying to understand how it worked and to predict how adjusting the various parameters would affect its behavior.

  I got technical, and pulled out my math:  I derived the Jacobian matrix, an array of partial derivatives of the system with respect to its state variables.   The Jacobean matrix was used in analytic solutions for linear systems, but for the less tractable non-linear system it wasn’t believed to be nearly so useful.  I programmed and calculated and graphed properties of the Jacobian called “eigenvalues” and “eigenvectors” in different places, and used that to try to predict how different chaotic behaviors might arrive.

 In short, I began to think I was learning something.

Silicon controlled rectifiers!) for various projects.   So it seemed natural that I got interested in the new home computer craze that began then.   Each month I eagerly awaited my Popular Science magazine, and  I read about the  Altair 8800 and IMSAI 8080.  I badly wanted one for myself.  Radio Shack came out with the TRS-80 and then personal computing really took off with the Commodore Pet.   And yet still hadn’t even seen a home computer up close - they were way too expensive for my family’s meager budget.

But about grade 8 I was introduced to computing at the QPS computer lab.  They had a weird Hewlett Packard “programmable calculator” that looked like a squashed home computer with a 2 line display.  You could program it in BASIC, either penciling in the dots on a series of punch cards, or if you were lucky, typing it in one line at a time on the computer’s keyboard.   Keyboard time was reserved for older students and in 15 minute slots.   I was jonesing for my first fix.

So I immediately started programming this relatively complicated game.  I quickly realized that I needed some trigonometry to work out where the tank would go and where the shots would go, based on the angle of travel.  I wrote out my BASIC program in long hand, dozens or hundreds of lines of code, then would go to the computer lab at my allotted time slot to feverishly type in as much as I could until my time ran out.  I could feel a thrum of energy in my body as I worked on the computer.  I had a blast working out how to graph the tank map and track where everything was.   I couldn’t wait to try it.
