I got into a rhythm, starting the wind tunnel each morning while I noshed on a croissant, then settled into a long spell of measuring.  I would position the instrument, allow the readings to settle, record the observation in a notepad,, and then repeat the procedure.  

Stephanopoulos went away for a few weeks to a conference to pick up new ideas.   I felt a tad abandoned, but decided to focus on my work.  By the time he was back, I surprised him by reporting the measurements were almost done.  He’d expected that I’d be at it all summer.

 Somewhat at a loss, he challenged me to analyze and tabulate the results, presenting them in the form of charts and graphs, and calculating  the characteristic exponent representing how the wind speed slowed toward the ground.  He explained the math that I would need to do.  It amounted to thousands of calculations.  Uggh.

These detailed calculations on hundreds of readings seemed like it would be even more tedious than taking the measurements.  I pondered this new assignment with dismay:  more tedium.  Not at all what I wanted.  So, reasoning that computers were good at this kind of repetitive work,  I decided to see if I could write a computer program to do all these calculations for me.  It might not be any faster than doing the calculations myself, but it promised to be more fun..  

My initial attempt was a bit disastrous:  the first version of the program was accidentally wiped out when the mainframe computer crashed before I had saved it..   I feverishly typed it back in before I forgot what I had written.  I pressed “save” …. And there was a pause.  Nothing happened.   As the moments passed, a hollow feeling formed in the pit of my stomach.  

Soon the ugly truth became clear:  The mainframe once again had gone down, taking my code with it.  The other denizens of the computer terminal room, each hunched over a large, dark terminal, were suddenly startled by a loud bang as I banged the metal cover of the computer terminal in frustration.  Heads flew around, and jaws dropped.  The sharp pain in my hand reflected the emotional pain of the loss of my work.

But over time I got the hang of saving my work as I went, and I soon made rapid progress.  By the end of the summer I’d fully analyzed the numbers that I’d collected, and generated a series of charts and graphs mapping and profiling the wind flow through the wind tunnel.

Very satisfied with this progress, Dr. Stephanopoulos asked me over the coming few summers to come back to work on ever more complex problems.  I would capture detailed observations about wind fluctuating wind pressures on model buildings.   And then I would write computer programs to analyze the work, looking at characteristics like the power spectrum and the distribution of peak pressures.  We identified that the most extreme (and dangerous) peak pressures occurred much more frequently than would be expected by traditional normal distributions, which meant that the existing building codes might not be sufficiently strong.

The work was challenging and rewarding, and my prof was pleased with my progress.  By the third year, my work resulted in enough answers to let us co-author a paper published in a refereed engineering journal. My chest puffed out in pride when I finally received a reprint of the paper.

***

After a couple of years at Harvard, I started doing some software there. I took a couple of computer science classes, including the notorious CS150, which dug into systems programming (tree  and hash indexes, assemblers, compression algorithms, etc.) in gory detail.  It was famously the hardest undergraduate course at Harvard, as voted year after year by student course evaluation surveys.

CS 150 involved a major project every week or two, in which students would need to learn a new skill (like a new programming language, or a new kind of algorithm) and then design, write, debug, and document a computer program to solve the given problem.  Each week there were six or eight hours of lectures and sections, followed by many hours of programing and debugging time, as we worked out how to tell this very stupid piece of equipment in precise detail how to do something rather clever.  Often I needed to work late into the evening on cramped terminals in the computer lab to complete the assignments.

  I enjoyed the intellectual challenge of the assignments, if not always the many, many hours of work.  But it was a great feeling to hand in a completed assignment with a working program that accomplished the stated goal.

Then  I got a part time job at Harvard Law Instruction (HLI) at the Harvard Law School.   HLI  was teaching law students about courtroom procedure by playing snippets of trials on video and asking them to object to impermissible cross-examination techniques.

The programming, in a language called Turbo Pascal was fast and simple, and Turbo was a cool interactive development environment that instantly took programmers right to the line of a bug.  Turbo was wonderful - but it was designed for small programs, and as ours grew ever larger and required a recompilation on each error, things began to bog down.   Working at HLI over the summer between junior and senior year, I took it upon myself to develop a kind of library linker, which allowed the large HLI libraries to be compiled once and then just linked in when compiling the code for each course.   This clever programming sped up the compile process by a factor of 10 or so for typical changes, making development significantly more efficient.  I thought that doing this might impress the (cute, female) president of HLI, but, alas, she had other priorities.

This project turned out to be a portent of things to come:  I took a piece of software that worked ok, but was a bit slow and clunky, dug down into its underpinnings to understand what was happening, then did some clever programming to speed it up by an order of magnitude, making it sing.   I found myself doing this again and again throughout my career as a programmer… that wasn’t my only programming trick, but probably the nerdiest.[p]

As I approached graduation, I realized that I didn’t want to continue on in Physics, at least not right away.  Physics in the US was contracting due to spending cuts as the cold war came to an end, and I wasn’t sure if I wanted to be an academic in a cost-cutting environment..   I kicked the tires of other career options - for instance I interviewed for a position as a consultant with McKinsey, but I wanted a position in Toronto and they had nothing there at the moment… by the time I decided I was willing to go to NYC, they had filled all their spots.  So I considered what else I might do, and programming seemed to be an option.

Jobs back home in Montreal seemed few, and preference was being given to people whose first language was French, so I decided to aim for a spot in Toronto.   Eventually, not long before graduation, I got a job working for the National Pacific Railway information technology department in Toronto, working on train dispatching and MIS projects.[q]

My first assignment was to work on an executive management information system.  We were supposed to make a graphical overview display showing train delays across the entire NP system - the Executive Work Station.  [r] A very clever programmer had written his own little programming language to make it easier to configure the displays, and the small team I was working on was using that programming language to code the displays.   As those displays became more and more complicated, we spent hours waiting for our programs to slowly compile.  These delays slowed our progress and threatened our deadline.[s]

I’d never written a compiler, but I had some idea about algorithms and data structures, so I dug into the compiler to see if I could optimize it.    First thing I noticed was that it used a linear symbol lookup, so as the program got bigger compiling slowed down dramatically.   A quick little hashtable sped up compiling by a huge factor.  Then with a few more tweaks, like switching from character input/output to block, I ended up speeding up the compiler by close to 10 times over the next couple of days.  This made our development fly, and let a colleague rapidly finish the rest of the development.  We rapidly refined the executive workstation, and the executives loved our work.   My nerd journey was well under way.[t]

***

Perhaps as a reward for my success with the Executive Work Station[u], my boss, Hugo, called me over one day and asked me if I could go to Montreal the next day.   Mystified, I asked why.   He told me that my colleague, Ian, was there working on debugging some safety critical train dispatching software during a “tiger team” testing session, and he was stressed out.  Could I go there, hold his hand, take him out to dinner when he needed it, and generally keep things on track?

I wasn’t at all sure I could do this. I hadn’t worked on the train dispatching software, and I wasn’t much of a people person.  I was a programmer.  But Hugo cajoled me, and told me how much it would help him and Ian.

So I showed up one cold fall morning in a large, dreary meeting room, filled with PCs and people,  in the basement of Metropolitan Station in Montreal.   Half a dozen railway operating managers (supervisors and senior dispatchers), mostly paunchy middle aged men, were banging away on the train dispatching software, and it wasn’t going well.  Everything they tried seemed to fail.  Ian, a twenty three year old engineer,  [v]was huddled in the corner, obviously stressed, speaking spasmodically in a loud uneven voice, then crouching over his computer. [w] Every few minutes someone would interrupt him to report a new problem and demand him to work on it.   No progress was being made, and the testers were grumbling.  One of them started suggesting that maybe we should cancel the whole session and reschedule it for a month or two later, once the software stabilized.   Ian seemed very alone and overwhelmed.[x]

I conferred with Hugo over the phone, asking what to do.   “Make it work,” he demanded. “I don’t care what you do, but don’t let them go home.  We can’t afford to postpone this.  It was too fucking hard to get everyone there!”[y]  My stomach tautened.

I conferred with[z] the senior tester.   How many bugs were there? I asked.  What were the biggest issues? I asked.    What was the top priority?

No one seemed to know.   All the testers knew was that it was bad.  And asking Ian just produced panic.  He complained that he couldn’t even reproduce most of the problems that were reported.

I asked the testers to stop talking directly to Ian[aa], to give him some breathing room to work on a bug, and to spend some time with me to tell me about the bugs they had seen[ab].   I started putting a list together.   Could they agree on what each bug was?  Could they reproduce them all?  

Eeryone agreed that the biggest problem was that when a dispatcher would enter in too many trains, the whole thing would crash, and that that[ac] was disastrous[ad].  And Ian had no idea how to fix that, because the computers were running out of memory, and there was no way to add more - the IBM PCs were at their maximum.  This problem threatened to bring everything down. .[ae]

I demanded that we put that one aside for the moment.   We were spinning our wheels [af]with the memory issue, so there was no point in working on it.  Let’s focus, I said,  on what we could do most effectively while everyone was there, and get Ian working on problems we knew how to solve.[ag]   I would think [ah]about the memory issue in the background, and maybe confer with some other software designers by phone.  Hopefully by the time we had knocked off the other big problems, we would have a strategy.[ai]

The testers reluctantly agreed to this approach.   Together we came up with something like 30 issues to work on.  For each issue, I asked one of the testers to take responsibility for coming up with a simple, reliable procedure for reproducing it.   Only the clearly reproducible bugs were sent to Ian for work … and even those were prioritized according to how important they were, and how easy they looked to fix.  

All of a sudden, the onus was back on the testers.  Now with something concrete to work on, something that they realized might help us make quicker progress, they got back to work seriously.   The grumbling and muttering came to an end as they rolled up their sleeves[aj]. Banging away on the computers, they started to  come up with test sequences  that would consistently and reliably trigger a bug. [ak] When they had one, they would show me, and I would congratulate them, and I would put that up on the list of bugs to be worked on by Ian.   The mood started shifting.  It started to feel like fun.[al]

After a few hours  we had half a dozen or a dozen reproducible bugs to work on, and not a lot of debugging progress to show - the bug list seemed very long.  But Ian had knocked off a couple of simpler bugs[am], and had started to settle down into productive work, coding happily.    Testers started testing Ian’s fixes, while others worked away trying to reproduce the more difficult to reproduce [an]problems, the ones that happened randomly.

Now each time Ian moved to a new bug, he had a tester who could show him exactly how to reproduce it.   Ian knew the software inside out, and half the time the second he saw the failure happen, he would shout out, “I know what’s happening!” while pointing his finger in the air.   He’d dive into the code, root about for a minute or two, make a couple of changes, recompile, and then try it again - often it was fixed on the first try.

Now the bugs were being knocked off the list rapidly …. The list of fixed bugs got longer and longer, and the list of open issues got shorter.    The excitement started to build.  Could we do this after all!?  

At the end of the first day I was there, we still had over a dozen repeatable issues to fix (plus more non-repeatable ones), but we’d knocked off nearly that many, and the increase in momentum was palpable.  When the testers were going home that evening there was hope in the air.[ao]

Ian and I worked late as he fixed bugs, then I eventually dragged him out of there, and through the deserted, cold streets of Montreal to find a busy restaurant to dine in.   Ian started to relax with the food and the warmth of the restaurant.  On the way home I stopped off to get some cash at an ATM.   It accidentally dispensed twice as much money as I asked for, but only debited my account for the money I asked for.[ap]  I thought about returning it, but there was no way, as everything was closed… so I kept it.  The free money seemed like a good omen.[aq]

The next day we got back to work and the progress was rapid.  Bugs were getting knocked off every few minutes, sometimes, and the “tiger team” tested happily.   In breaks, the testers told each other stories about working on the railway - outsize personalities, bad weather, wrecks, drinking binges, and so on.  The tension ebbed.

But the out of memory issue continued to haunt everyone.   A couple of the testers had come up with a standard, consistent  test sequence that would reliably trigger the bug after around 6-8 trains had been entered.   We needed to be able to handle at least 15-20 trains at once.   And there was no way to add more memory.  We had hit the dreaded 640KB limit that IBM PCs had in those early days of personal computers.[ar]

I asked if we could do something to make the software smaller, so that there was more space in the memory for train data.   Maybe we could use something called overlays, where bits of the program were swapped out onto disk so that not all had to be in memory at once?

Everybody said that this wouldn’t work - the software needed to be very fast, and the users couldn’t wait while things loaded.  Both the testers and Ian agreed vigorously.  We needed a different solution.

I kept thinking about this, in the gaps between updating the bug list and chatting with the testers and congratulating Ian on his latest victory.    I looked to see if there were any ways to make the compiler squeeze the software, or to remove anything unnecessary from the software.  Not much seemed plausible.

Eventually I realized that there were a few user operations that didn’t happen very often.  For instance, printing up a train sheet was something done once a shift, and it took a minute or two.   Wouldn’t it be ok if that took a few seconds longer, while the software loaded?

I played with the overlays idea a bit, telling the linker to put the printing logic in the same memory area as some startup logic, so that the startup logic would be swapped out when the printing was happening.  Would the software still run?   Would there be any performance problems?  Would it improve the number of trains we could load?[as]

By this point Ian had knocked off many of the biggest bugs.  The ones that were left were mostly nuisance issues.  Except for the memory[at] issue.   The testers were starting to come up for air, and to wonder a little cynically what we would do about the memory problem.[au]

After playing for a while with the link map to put in a couple of overlays, I asked a couple of testers if they could try out my idea.   They entered a few trains, and it worked.  They printed out a train sheet.  There was a slight but barely noticeable pause, and it printed out correctly.  So far so good.   They entered six trains, and it was fine.  Then a couple of more, and it kept on working - we had gone farther than before.  The testers chattered happily..  Then another, and they cheered a little.. More of the testers gathered around to watch.   It felt like people were holding their breath, collectively.  Then at 10, it failed, and there was a groan…. It wasn’t enough, but it was the first real, meaningful progress we’d made, and it felt exciting.  Everyone agreed that the performance was fine.   There were some smiles, and a few high fives … even Ian, who had come over in the middle of this,  looked excited and optimistic.  His skepticism had vanished.[av]

Ian took over from me, playing with the loader overlay map like a confident maestro, using his deep knowledge of how the software worked, and his new understanding of how overlays could help.   After an hour or two of compiling and overlay testing and debugging,  we ran another big test, while everyone watched…. The tension mounted as train after train was added, and tested   … a huge cheer erupted when we hit 15, and then only a mild groan and some laughter when the software immediately crashed.   And after some more brainstorming and a few more hours of tweaking, Ian consistently got over  20 trains to work correctly, which was well over the requirement.   We had made it!   The only remaining issues were little nuisances that were simple but tedious to fix, things like formatting printouts more cleanly.  Ian was elated, the testers were full of smiles, and I was hugely relieved that this surprising few days that looked so unpromising had come out so well.[aw]

Ian and I flew home to the congratulations of Hugo and the rest of the team.

***[ax]

After these successes, Hugo assigned me to work on train dispatching software.

For a while I worked on the system that I had helped Ian debug in Montreal that day.   It was used for dispatching branch lines and spurs, areas where it wasn’t worth putting in full signaling systems.  Once computerization was added, those branches became much more productive, so there was a big pressure to add it everywhere that wasn’t signaled (or, as they called it on the railroad, dark territory.)  [ay]

After this success, we were given a chance with the big leagues, centralized traffic control or CTC,  which was used to control the main line, places where the overhead signals controlled the train flow.

Hugo sold the business on the idea of developing our own CTC control workstation using PCs with large, Mitsubishi or NEC monitors with high resolution Matrox graphics cards.  At the time this was very high technology, with over $10,000 worth of display technology per workstation, what seemed like an enormous amount of money.  But each workstation would replace a $100,000 manually wired wall-sized track display, and it would allow one dispatcher to more easily manage several subdivisions, without moving around.   This could help save money by reducing the number of dispatchers on off-peak times.[az]

Hugo started setting up the large, complicated project..   He had been assigned some project leaders from the mainframe development group, and broke the project into several teams.   My team worked on some of the low level system programming, like interfacing with the Matrox card and the CTC.  Another team, led by Ron Piggott, a mainframe project leader who was a whiz with Cobol,  worked on the user interface.  Others worked on the routing, the mainframe database interface, and other problems.[ba]

My team did its job, and so did the others, but the user interface team had highly visible problems.  Its displays worked correctly, but they refreshed excruciatingly slowly.  Whenever we demoed the system to users, like dispatchers and superintendents, they shook their heads and said that it wouldn’t work in real life.   They talked about how much faster the (much cruder and more expensive) displays from the signals companies worked, and how quickly dispatchers needed to be able to respond..  Meanwhile, political conflicts between the different team leaders festered, particularly between the mainframe people and the PC people.[bb]

This dragged on and on, and it put the project at risk - clearly we weren’t making the performance needs of the business problem.    I complained in project meetings that Ron Piggott’s team was using floating point math - and that this ran much, much slower on PCs of the day than integer math.   I told them they were going to need to switch to integer.   Ron resisted, saying that doing this would take weeks to rewrite the software and cause his team to miss its deadline.   And besides, there was no guarantee that this would fix the problem.   The displays continued to lag.[bc]

Eventually, frustrated by the lack of understanding by Ron and the lack of support from Hugo, I took matters into my own hands.   Staying late after work one Friday night, I started programming a demo in the C programming language, written using integer math, with most of the calculations offloaded to the powerful Matrox graphics board.  I worked in a frenzy, noticing how quickly and smoothly my demo was going.   I worked hour after hour, eventually staying until Saturday morning.  By the time I was done, I had a demo that would zoom from a full subdivision view with a dozen stations, all the way into a single station siding view, refreshing as fast as a user could press the “zoom” key.  It flew like the wind.[bd]

Monday morning I showed Hugo my demo.   He hummed appreciatively. He called Ron over, who watched it, looking green with disgust and dismay.  

There was a huge eclat in the project.   Once they saw the changes, the end users were delighted. Ron’s team had to rewrite their software according to my ideas.  Ron stopped speaking to me, and glared at me every time he saw me.   I savored my triumph, but I also noticed the tension, and how it tightened my belly to be around Ron.[be]

That project continued to have problems.   There were conflicts between the client side team and the server team.  Things didn’t connect.  Schedules didn’t align.   People talked about other people behind their backs.   I felt more and more stressed.

Eventually I took matters into my own hands again, with my first foray into addressing office politics.   I somehow decided to try openness, in my own inimitable style.

I ended up writing an email in the form of a project newsletter.  It was called “All the project dirt.”  And it dished the dirt.  It shared all the conflicts, and backstabbing, and disagreements, and failures.   It put it all into light.  It named names, and highlighted faults and mistakes.

And in a fit of brilliance, one evening I sent this loaded missive to the entire project team.

To my surprise, the project team’s reaction to this flash of brilliant light was not what I expected.   Instead of everybody reacting with relief that someone had finally said the emperor had no clothes, I found myself in a grim and silent office the next day.  No one said a word.  No one looked at me.

Eventually Hugo called me into his office.  He was curious, he said, looking at “All the project dirt” on his computer, what had caused me to write all of this, and to decide to share it with the whole project team.   He didn’t deny that any of it was true, but he asked me to consider how sharing the steamy load of ickiness might have made the other project leaders feel, and how it might have affected their ability to lead their teams.   He asked me to consider whether what I had done was helpful.  As he haltingly talked about this, I noticed that he was slumped down and looked defeated and sad.

This was something I hadn’t considered.   I had assumed that, like with programming, once we all understood the problem, the solutions would become obvious.   I thought that shedding light on and airing the conflicts and issues would somehow blow off the stink.   I hadn’t understood that office politics work differently.  I noticed, as I sat across from Hugo, a hotness on my face and a smallness in my own body, a weight on my shoulders and a tension on my brow.  It was at this moment that I realized that I had fucked up.

Shortly thereafter I was reassigned to a new, much smaller project.

***

By this point lunch was prepared and we all sat down to enjoy it.   Dan continued his line of questioning:   “Ok,” he said, “I got that you were a nerd who was incompetent with office politics.  But you never answered about how you met Mary.  What kind of magic put the two of you together?   Did your fairy godmother send her to you?”

“Umm, not exactly my fairy godmother,” said I, “but maybe something along those lines. Let me tell you.”

Chapter 9 : Relating

The summer of 1985, after graduating Harvard,  I moved to Toronto to start work at NP Rail.   I moved into the basement of a beautiful 1888 Victorian townhouse in an area that was then called South Riverdale (and now Riverside), sharing a flat with a cynical psych nurse named Annette.[bf]

The first few months were an adjustment - getting used to taking streetcars to work, figuring out how to feed myself, learning about living with a cranky roommate, and so on.   I didn’t know a lot of people my age, but my parents had a number of friends in the Toronto area.   [bg]

One of those families included a young woman a couple of years older than I was, and who was my parents’ goddaughter.  I met Justine at some kind of a family function; she was petite and very cute, with short hair and a nice, shy smile.  I was interested.

One day Justine invited me to a Tafelmusik concert.  Tafelmusik was a Baroque ensemble playing on early instruments, and I had told Justine that at Harvard I had done a course on concertos and other ensemble forms in the Baroque and classical eras,[bh] and I had formed quite a liking for Handel’s concerti grossi.  So I was curious to go, and to get to know Justine better.   Would I mind, she asked, if she brought a friend?[bi]

Come concert night, we three met.  I was smitten with Justine - she was engaging and fun to tease.  Her friend, Mary, was shy at first and a little awkward and I found her nowhere near as cute as Justine.   We enjoyed the concert, so full of lively and energetic music,  short pieces with lots of variety,  so different from my prior experience of a symphony hall, and afterwards headed to a nearby Chinese restaurant to eat and talk more.

At the restaurant I peacocked a little, unconsciously, picking up a menu and using my newly acquired Harvard critical thinking skills to do an analysis of the restaurant’s focus and target audience.  How had they put the menu together?  What was its graphic design?  Were they targeting students or wealthy residents of the Annex?  I was trying to show that in university [bj]I had learned to think analytically and critically about everything - not to take anything at face value.  Justine and Mary were slightly baffled at my fact-free disquisition on Chinese restaurant marketing but smiled and played along.

Justine was curious about my time in the States.   Wasn’t it terrible, she wondered, to have to be in the US so long?   Wasn’t it awful to be away from Canada, where the people were so much kinder?  Weren’t the Americans so much more biased than Canadians?

Now, it happened that at some level I agreed with some of Justine’s biases.  I did find American prejudices a bit hard to take at times.  But Justine’s own level of ignorant prejudice was too tempting a target for me to skip.[bk]   I started into a rapturous paean to American spirit, to their can-do attitude, and I punctured Justine’s preconceptions with pleasure and elan, smiling as I teased her about her attitudes.   Justine looked a little mortified, and I barely noticed how Mary was laughing gaily at her friend’s discomfiture.   We headed home feeling like we’d had a fun evening.

The next day I screwed up my courage and phoned Christine and asked her out again.   She thanked me and let me know that she wasn’t available for the next while but maybe there would be a time when she wasn’t so busy.   I was dismayed but vowed to call her again in a few weeks.  These things take time, after all.

Then the following day, I received a phone call.  It was Mary on the other end of the line.   I was stunned, tongue-tied.  I stammered hello.    I’d never had a woman call me out of the blue.  After some very long, painful moments of awkward, desultory conversation (it would seem that Mary was not particularly glib in these matters, either), she came out with it:  She had enjoyed herself the other night and wondered if I would see her again.[bl]

“You mean, just with you? “  I stammered. “Not with Justine?”[bm]

There was a long pause.  No, not with Justine, she confirmed.  Just her.    

I was baffled.  I hadn’t even noticed this woman, I’d barely even spoken to her, I wasn’t at all interested, and here she was asking me out.   For a long moment, I wasn’t sure what to say.   Did I want to see her again?  Eventually I decided, what the hell.  I guess I could go out and see what happened.  It couldn’t be any worse than my current situation.  It wasn’t like I had a long line of women beating at my door asking for dates.

So after a long pause, and some “Erms….” and “Umms….” I said, “Um… sure, I guess so.  What do you have in mind?”

She had in mind a movie - perhaps something a little meaningful, romantic.   Agnes of God was playing, and would I enjoy seeing it?

We met to see the movie, and ninety minutes of spooky religious stories featuring dead babies and off kilter nuns and debatable miracles spooled by, leaving us stunned and baffled.   But after some minutes of plodding along in awkward, dispirited silence towards home, we started to voice our opinions on the movie.   There was an edge to some of Mary’s comments, wry, thoughtful, and funny observations on the plot and the themes.   I responded with my own take [bn]on it … We enjoyed this funny conversation about this terrible first date movie.

We continued to meet up, to see movies and play and to go to concerts.  And to wittily analyze them afterwards.   It transpired that Mary was studying music education, after completing a music degree at University of Toronto.

Not much later, Mary’s sorority (or as they preferred, women’s fraternity) held a toga party, in conjunction with a neighboring fraternity.  Would I like to come?

I wasn’t too keen on university parties, but this sounded different from anything I’d been to.  It had a kind of sexy edge.

On the day of the party I arrived at the sorority house.   It was full of students, female and male, coursing through the building, yelling over the loud music, drinking beer, and leering at each other.   I met Mary, who was wearing a clumsy toga pinned over a T-shirt and shorts and together we headed over to the male fraternity house.

There the action was even louder and more crowded and more beer sodden.    The smells of beer and sweaty students permeated the air.   The floor was sticky.   We each had a drink. Then I had another.  I lost track of Mary’s count, and also of mine….   There was noise.  There were introductions to people whom I would never meet again.  There was not a lot of actual sexiness in the toga costumes, just people wearing bedsheets over clothing and squeezed close together and yelling at each other.  There was squeezing through crowds of people to get drinks or pick up winter clothing.    

After a time we headed back to Mary’s sorority house.  We met more people, including Justine, who it turned out was a sorority sister of Mary’s.   (I later learned that Justine was Mary’s student mentor at her sorority, or as they called it, “sister-mother”.  That meant that my mother’s god-daughter - Justine - was Mary’s sister-mother, so in the following years we would tell people that Mary was my mother’s god-daughter's sister-daughter, which generally baffled them.)[bo]

Mary invited me to see her bedroom, which was shared with a roommate, who was out at the party.  She closed the door.  Would I like to lie on the bed with her?  Did I want to kiss her? Did I want help with loosening my shirt?

Next thing I knew,[bp] I was naked on top of her, also nake[bq]d, attempting intercourse in a half darkened room, the noises of the party seeming distant.   I could dimly feel the warmth of her body underneath me, her breasts squeezing into my chest, the compression of fitting into a narrow bed causing pressure as my groin encountered her hips.  It was a surprising development, but exciting.  While the spirit was willing to go ahead with this intention, with all the alcohol and the unexpected movement to bed, there was panic inside.  How would this all work out?   The flesh was having a difficult time supporting the objective, despite the pleasant warmth of the skin-on-skin contact.  I fumbled, trying to raise some interest from my recalcitrant member, attempting to penetrate the dark interior of Mary’s secret cavern. Eventually, after some considerable effort to get my member to come out to play, I managed a slight, sloppy ingress.

At this moment the door suddenly burst open and light and noise from the party rushed into the room.  A tall, dark-haired young woman in a toga swept in, peered into the gloom, and emitted a startled, “Oh … who’s there?”

Mary calmly and politely gestured from below me, “Hi Jane, this is Bobby.  Bobby, this is my roommate, Jane.”

Jane took in the situation, a naked man lying atop her naked roommate [br]during a raucous fraternity party,  closed her gaping mouth, and said  “Hi!  ..… I mean, oops, I’m so sorry.”  Putting her hands to her flaming cheeks, she rapidly turned on her heel and fled, slamming the door behind her.  My half filled member fell out of its newly won position and I slumped against Mary, my face aflame with embarrassment.  I was mortified, wondering how I could have chosen to take advantage of poor Mary in such a crude and inappropriate way, embarrassing her in front of her roommate.  I closed my eyes, trying to work out what to do next, how to extricate myself gracefully.

“Don’t stop”, said Mary.

***

The toga party led to a new era in Bobby Lindo’s life.  I was assuredly no longer a virgin.   And by dint of this act of sexual congress, and by the fact that Mary and I kept seeing each other, I was, implicitly, In A Relationship.   I eventually figured out that the Tafelmusik concert with Justine had been a setup - Justine had pegged me as a fit for Mary, and had told Mary but had chosen not to tell me.  I guess she assumed I had some kind of social awareness and therefore would guess.

Mary and I didn’t talk a lot about The Relationship, nor that much about each other …. We did tell each other a very little about our sexual histories (I worked out that I wasn’t Mary’s first, nor probably even her second, but I was too shy to delve further), but we were much more interested in each other’s opinions on things:   on music and drama and film and novels, in particular.

We started attending Tafelmusik regularly, in time becoming long term subscribers.  We tried a few other early music groups, including the Toronto Consort, a group specializing in Medieval and Renaissance music.  We went to the occasional opera.  We went to Stratford to see Shakespearian plays.  We visited trendy restaurants.

For Christmas I bought Mary several cookbooks, including The Complete Asian Cookbook, which had hundreds of recipes from over a dozen countries.   That book turned out to be a revelation:   From that book we discovered several things, including that we loved Southeast Asian cuisine, and that we loved working together to cook it.   We cooked dozens of meals with food from all over the area:  Szechuan China, Thailand, Malaysia, Indonesia, India, and others.  We dreamed of visiting the area.[bs]

At Christmas time we would go to a timeshare resort in the Laurentians and cook Indian food and set off the smoke detectors and fuck.  When we talked of the future, it would be about what films to see next, and whether to go to Stratford next summer.

***

The summer of 1988 we went to Stratford again.  The productions we saw that year were not as memorable as previous years.    In previous years there had been a fun Rosencrantz and Guildenstern are Dead that was produced alongside a Hamlet, so you could see the same story from both points of view with the same actors and costumes on the same visit, and an amazing production of Pericles that made a mediocre play seem quite inspiring.  That year all that was memorable was a production of Taming of the Shrew set in a funky 1950s Italy.[bt]

But something more memorable did happen … at the Church restaurant, which was, as one might expect from the name, a restaurant in a renovated church, after dinner and a corked bottle of wine, I pulled out a cheap costume jewelry ring and awkwardly proposed to Mary, telling her I would buy her a proper engagement ring once she told me what she wanted.   She accepted my proposal immediately[bu].

The decision to propose had been a challenging one.  I noticed that I didn’t really love Mary, at least not in the sense of butterflies in the stomach and the feeling of sweetness and light when I was around her.   But I liked her and loved our friendship, our easy companionship and wordplay and shared love of culture and cooking.   And I didn’t trust love, or at least what I understood of love, and that I didn’t trust that love would last through a marriage.  Friendship and compatibility, I thought, was a better bet for a long term relationship, than risky and volatile romantic love, which likely would not last.

And I felt that if we didn't marry, Mary would simply move along and find someone who would marry her.  And I would be left alone.  And after my two year dry spell at Harvard, I didn’t fancy my chances for finding another partner.   I assumed that I would be alone forever.  I felt that I really had little choice but to marry her.

It’s interesting as I look back to note that I never discussed most of this with Mary.   I didn’t ask her what she wanted.  I didn’t tell her about my fears.   In short, I didn’t really connect.  I projected what she must feel, and acted on my projections and assumptions.   This was to turn into a pattern.[bv]

***

Once we got engaged, things moved pretty quickly.  We moved in together within a few weeks … My roommate Annette had decided to move back home to New Brunswick, and I took over her lease and Mary moved in with me.  And around the same time we both made career shifts.  Then in November we were married, in a small ceremony in Toronto followed by a reception in a boutique hotel downtown.

For our honeymoon, we waited until the next summer, when Mary would be able to get time off from teaching, and took a five week trip to Asia - Thailand, Bali, Hong Kong, and parts of Japan.  

The trip was the trip of a lifetime - we found that the cultures of the places we visited were incredibly different from one another, more different than European countries were from each other.  We loved the architecture and the people watching, and especially the food.  In Thailand we took cooking classes at the Oriental Hotel in Bangkok, and visited the best Thai restaurants in town.   We learned about the northern style and the southern style of Thai architecture.  We were scammed by a lovely, highly educated “tour guide” and we loved it.  In Bali we learned about Balinese New Year’s celebrations, which come five times a year.   In Japan we visited a mountain resort that reminded us of Jasper, Alberta.  Every day brought something new and amazing.[bw]

Mary shifted over to teaching high school students, first as a long term occasional teaching business and computer, then as a music teacher in northern Scarborough.  We settled into married life.   In a couple of years, around 1990 or 1991, the house two doors down went on the market, and we bought it, staying in the same row of 1888 Victorian townhouses, but getting a corner lot with a little yard.  We continued to live on the ground floor and in the basement, and we rented out the top two floors.   I assumed we would continue to build our marriage, one commitment at a time.[bx]

Shortly after moving into the new home, Mary spoke to me one night at bedtime, with this proposal:[by]  Now that we had our marriage and our home, which were fixing up, and she had the job she wanted, wouldn’t it be great to have a family?   We could have kids, and in time take over the whole house to have to raise them.  She would be able to have kids who could love her, and she could love them.

On the face of it, this was a totally reasonable suggestion.  We had stable income, a home, and we were building a life together … and while we hadn’t discussed this in so many words when we were married, I think we both assumed we would have a couple of kids, just like our parents had, and so many people we knew.

This time, however, something unexpected happened:   Contrary to my expectation, when Mary asked about having kids, I had an immediate and visceral reaction:  No way!   I had all kinds of reasons that this wasn’t the time:   I wasn’t ready, it was too early, I wasn’t sure I’d be a good father, any kid of mine would probably be a pain in the ass to raise, given my own history, we might want to do other things first, etc., etc.    Mary reluctantly accepted my arguments, but I felt guilty.  Shouldn’t I want to have children?  Was there something wrong with me?

An argument that I didn’t fully make - I raised but withdrew - was that I was suspicious of Mary’s motives for having children.   I didn’t have a problem with having children so that we could raise a new generation of people to carry life forward.  But to have children in order to have someone to love us?  Well, it seemed to me that there was no guarantee that the children would feel that way, and I didn’t want to raise kids with the expectation that they needed to do that.  Something felt wrong about that, something a bit needy.   Having kids and hoping that they would be there for us in our old age, no problem with that.  But creating new lives in order to have someone to love us - that was a step too far for my taste.[bz]

And while the other direction, loving one’s kids, was certainly a laudable goal of parenthood, I saw raising children as a huge responsibility, and that it wasn’t clear that doing it in order to have someone to love was enough:   more like it should be the other way around, that you need to love your kids to have the determination to raise them.  Because I expected that raising children would be a challenge, and not necessarily enjoyable all the time.

I didn’t like either of these arguments very much.  It felt like I was trying to belittle Mary’s desire to have kids by coming up with spurious counter-arguments of my own.  It felt that I was being selfish, while she was the one with more compassion and caring.  And that my concerns about her motives were a bit shameful, not to be spoken aloud.  But in any event we agreed to defer the decision.

I carried this guilt and shame around for a couple of years, as we both navigated career transitions and went through the business of life: planting a garden, traveling, etc.

[ chapter break?]

Finally something changed:  In the fall of 1994 I was given an exciting job opportunity in New York City, and Mary would not be able to work there for a couple years until she could get her papers.   I suggested that she could use the opportunity to compose music, which was something she had been talking about for years.   But she didn’t seem at all enthusiastic.   Trying to figure out a carrot to get Mary to be excited about the new opportunity, I thought about the idea of having children.  Could I imagine myself as a dad?  Could it work out?

The stars seemed aligned:  I would be making a great income, Mary wouldn’t be working, and we would be in a new place where we could spend time making a family.  We could come back in a few years and settle back into Toronto.   So there were lots of great, logical reasons for having kids.   Could I swallow my misgivings?

Eventually, I decided I could.  I suggested to Mary that this might be a good time to have kids, and she was delighted with the idea.   And I found myself feeling excited - about the new job in NYC, about moving, and for the prospect of being a father.   So many things changing, all at once![ca]

Mary quickly went off birth control, and we settled into a new routine:  She had just started a new teaching contract in Scarborough, and would not be able to move to NYC until next summer.  So each week or two I would hop on a plane on a Sunday evening and head to Manhattan.  I would work there for a week and then come back to Toronto Friday afternoon.  Sometimes, instead, Mary would come to visit me in New York for the weekend, as we apartment hunted.

The first day after work in the new job, which was in a building next to the Chrysler building and across from Grand Central on Lexington, I headed south on Lex just to see what I could see.   New York City was interesting, and this was largely away from the most touristy areas.   As I headed south of 23rd St., the character of the area changed - there was a mixture of brownstones and  several storey apartment buildings of varied height, all looking urbane in a kind of timeworn way, and a beautiful tiny square enclosed by an iron fence, Gramercy Park, which I later learned was locked and only for the neighbours.  But the area was beautiful, and I was hooked … particularly when I headed down Irving Place, the extension of Lexington, and stopped for dinner at Pete’s Tavern, a pub with a literary history.   I tucked into a strip loin steak and decided that I wanted to leave nearby.  I hadn’t expected anything so cozy in NYC.

Our new routine was exciting but stressful, and eventually we slowed the pace of our visits back and forth a little, sometimes taking a weekend apart to avoid the crush of Laguardia airport and the cab or limo rides to and from the airport.

At Christmas time, Mary came to Manhattan for a longer stretch, to stay with me at my temporary apartment and get to know our new home city a little better.   We went to the Gramercy Tavern, a high end restaurant with a multi-course tasting menu, ending with a beautiful, elaborate cheese course.    That night, in the middle of the night, Mary was up vomiting:   The beautiful food was too rich.   [cb]

But the vomiting persisted the next day, and she confessed:  She’d missed her period, and this was perhaps actually morning sickness.  She was pregnant!  She went back home with a new life growing in her, both of us excited to see what would happen.

While she was there, we had found our first permanent home in New York, a second storey apartment overlooking East 23rd Street, a few blocks from Gramercy Park itself, and on the edge of the Gramercy Park area of NYC.  It wasn’t quite as nice a location as Gramercy itself, but it was cheek by jowl, and good enough for me.  We’d also considered Brooklyn Heights (too expensive and too far from work) and Park Slope (lovely but way too far on the F train).[cc]  We started making plans to move to 23rd St.

At spring break, Mary was back in Manhattan… when one evening things felt off for her, and then she began to cramp … then to bleed.  Eventually she passed a large lump of organic matter, looking rather like a fetus.  The bleeding didn’t stop.  I began to worry as the colour drained from her.

With Mary’s resulting anemia, there was no immediate possibility of trying again to get pregnant.  She finished out the school year, then I came back to Toronto to finalize Mary’s move down to New York.

That summer, the summer of 1995, I worried for the first time about our marriage.  Mary was depressed and lethargic, with little will to take on anything new.   There was no spark.  Our connection seemed limited.  We didn’t talk with each other about the miscarriage, or how we felt about it.   We didn’t talk about the future.  I wondered where we would go next, how we would make this work.

And stayed that way for quite some time… the beeping of the pulse oximeter showed that the pressure [cf]was taking a toll, that the baby was starting to have oxygen deprivation as the umbilical cord got squeezed in the narrow passageway.  The machines beeped warnings ominously and insistently.  The doc started talking about a C-section, to protect the baby.

Or maybe not, because suddenly all of the delivery room personnel were commenting on how unusual that this was - a laughing, pink, beautiful newborn.  It felt that we were off to a good start, after a dangerous-seeming delivery.

Mother and child repaired to the recovery room and mother had a mug of Guinness to celebrate, and to rebuild her enzymes.   We commented that for the rest of her life, Guinness would taste like mother’s milk to Juliet.

Nothing.   What was going on?   We searched about.  Nothing on the blankets, no leakages.  Finally we followed our noses back to the pram, and noticed the stinky cheese smell growing.  Aha!   We reached in and pulled out …. The package of époisses cheese, a stinky  French soft cheese fermented in Marc de Bourgogne (a kind of Burgundian eau-de-vie), was emanating its characteristic rotten milk fug.  We had tucked it in next to Juliet, and in the warmth it had contributed its unique aroma to the carriage.

In 1996 we moved from our rented apartment on East 23rd Street to a coop apartment just off University Place on East 9th Street in the NYU area of Greenwich Village, just north of Washington Square park.    When Juliet was teething and couldn’t sleep, I’d tak[ci]e her out in her stroller and head down towards TriBeCa… the cobblestone streets and sidewalks would jostle and jounce the stroller vigorously, soon putting her to sleep.

East 9th was good to us.  We had a one bedroom apartment converted into a 2, with a huge butcher block counter surrounding the kitchen and dominating the living room and dining room.  We were just over a wonderful steakhouse and a tasty barbecue restaurant, and steps from a corner store, dry cleaner, and a variety of Italian restaurants.  A short distance further put us at all kinds of services - restaurants of all kinds, the lovely SoHo area with its kitchen stores and vintage furniture stores, and so on.  Juliet had a toddler friend who lived downstairs, and Mary found a group of women to do African drumming with.  Soon we had visiting West African drummers coming by, and djembe drums and goat skin hides stored in our closets.

Within a couple of years Mary was jonesing for another child.  Life was pretty good in Manhattan, but stressful - my work kept me busy many hours a day, and Mary had help from a mother’s helper to take care of Juliet and the business of life.   Mary wanted more, and soon she had it - she was ballooning with another child, ballooning to the point of concerns about her health.

While Mary was with child, we were looking for a country place to buy outside of New York City.  We put in offers on a few places in the Poconos, eventually closing on a property south of the Ashokan reservoir, about twenty five minutes from Woodstock, and just over two hours from New York City.

Brad was born around the winter solstice, a few days before Christmas of 1998.   We trundled him off to the Harvard Club for a stylish Christmas dinner with his sister and grandparents.   Next day he started coughing and sniffling … within a few days he had taken a turn for the worse, and one evening a few days before New Year’s we rushed him to Emergency at St. Vincent’s hospital nearby, his lips blue, fighting for breath.   The staff there seemed disinterested in our panic, demanding that we fill out a form and wait our turn.  But the magic words “blue lips” on the intake form got their attention, and Brad was instantly rushed into the ER itself and under an oxygen mask.

Brad spent the next three weeks there, in a special isolation ward on the pediatric wing, attached to heart monitors and respiratory apparatus.   He fought for breath, gasping with effort, for close to two weeks … the doctors admired his energy and seemed sanguine about his prospects, reassuring his terrified parents that all would be well, that it would just take time.  Apparently he’d picked up a case of RSV, probably from us … RSV, we learned, is a respiratory virus that affects newborns, especially preemies, much more strongly than adults; in an adult it presents as the common cold. And we’d had the sniffles before Brad was born.

New Year’s Eve Mary and I sat vigil over Brad’s bed in the pediatric ward at the hospital, his grandparents looking after Juliet.   As the evening wore on towards midnight, the hospital, normally active with “code blue” and “code white” announcements, gradually fell silent.  There was an eerie silence for the thirty minutes before midnight.   At midnight there was a very quiet cheer from the nursing staff in the distance, then nothing…  then at 12:05 am, there was an announcement on the PA system, a code called to prepare for an incoming ambulance.  Then another at 12:07.  Then more.   By 12:10 it was busier than we'd ever heard it before.  By 12:15 or 12:20 it was mayhem, with code blues and code whites and code reds and stats echoing through the halls, and the sound of running feet and rattling beds and gurneys passing the door of Brad’s room every few seconds.  The sudden surge in activity was shocking.  We wondered if there had been a terrorist act, or a major disaster on the subway.

Eventually a nurse stopped by to check in on Brad.   “What was the disaster?”, we asked.     The nurse looked confused.  What were we talking about?   We described the extreme activity in the hospital, after the pronounced lull.

“Oh,” the nurse said.  “Right, I guess that’s confusing.   It happens every year.  For the few minutes before midnight, everybody is holding their breath waiting for the countdown.  The minute it hits midnight, drunk people start to head to their next destination.   There are accidents.  Fights break out.  We get busy.”  She shrugged,  “I guess I’d better get going and make up some beds. There are probably going to be a few kids to look after later tonight.”

Lunch was winding down and people were starting to talk about going back to the hot tub.[cj]

Monica commented on my story.  “Wow,” she said, “The first couple of years in New York sounded pretty intense.  How did you decide to go there?  What was your job?  Did you get a transfer?”

Chapter 10: Working Life[ck][cl][cm]

After all the conflict at NP, I decided I hated office politics and wanted to go out on my own.   I made a deal with my brother-in-law, Pete, a small land developer, to help me set up a small software consulting firm to advise small businesses on software installations.   I started to work at this, with little success.  

Meanwhile, Pete asked me to help take a look at his balance sheet and cash flow to see if I could make any suggestions on how to run his business a bit tighter.

Digging into the numbers, I was stunned.   Pete’s assets were dwarfed by his liabilities, and he had negative cash flow on essentially all of his properties.  His only hope was to somehow make a killing by developing some of his properties, but the banks wouldn’t lend him money to do this, because they sensed his poor financial condition.   I told Pete that he was losing money on everything he did, but making it up on volume.  Somehow the joke wasn’t very funny.

I quickly decided that I needed to find a more stable income.  So I reached out to a head hunter and got myself a job working for Canadian Nuclear Power Ltd.  (CNPL) t[cn]o help them craft their response to regulators, who after the recent Chernobyl disaster were questioning CNPL about their safety systems.  My experience in safety critical train dispatching software at NP was helpful, as was my Harvard writing background.

I worked at CNPL for several years, first working with engineers to draft responses to regulators, then actually designing a new generation of shutdown software. [co] (My design was made to make its behaviour very easy to review, by putting all of the parameters into easily readable tables, and simplifying the code into a few simple loops to go through the parameter tables.   This design, influenced by the work I had done at NP, was deemed insufficiently “provable” by Ontario Power management, the clients for the work.  Basically, there was nothing to prove.  So instead, under the influence of a professor of computer science,  a much more complex design with thousands of lines of code and tens of thousands of lines of proof was chosen.   I was disgusted, believing that a proof that complex was more likely to be in error than my very simple design.)

After a few years, I managed to get myself fired (or, rather, my contract not renewed) by sending promotional materials about my work at CNPL to senior CNPL managers.    I’m not exactly sure what I was hoping to achieve by this, but I managed to piss a few people off.  Once again my judgment on office politics was suspect.[cp]

After CNPL, I worked from home on my own software project for a while, an office automation tool that combined elements of a spreadsheet and database and word processor.   It was complex, and eventually I realized that people preferred to buy separate packages and link the results together.  So I went looking for office work again.[cq]

Eventually, around 1992, I took a job working for the cellular telephone company Cellular Canada… I was impressed by the number of women in the lobby of the office where I interviewed, and that my manager was to be a strong and smart woman.

Cellular turned out to be a bit of mes[cr]s … It turned out that I was to report to a different person than my offer letter promised, also a woman but whom I found much less impressive.  We were doing snazzy work with Object Oriented Design at a time when OOD was still pretty new.  We were linking together different methods to try to come up with an overall methodology, which was exciting, but the working environment was a bit fraught.   After a year or so I started looking for a new job.

By late summer of 1994, I was interviewing for work in the financial industry with Imperial Canadian Bank, a Toronto-based bank.  The work was to be either in Toronto or New York.   After a screening interview in Toronto they flew me down to NYC on a hot early September day, to be subjected to an intense round of interviews, around 10 in total in one day.  Some of the interviews were with traders; they would wander in, say hi, ask about how my day was going, nod vaguely at my answers,  and wander off after a minute or so, looking bored.   It seemed unpromising,  but shortly thereafter, a letter came: they wanted me to report for work in New York in a few weeks, at a starting salary plus bonus about triple my current compensation.

Late October of 1994 I flew down to New York City one Monday morning, to report to work.  By 9 am when I arrived at the office I’d been up for nearly 5 hours and had spent much of that in transit.  The scene that greeted me was surprising:  a large, nearly empty trading floor,[cs] with a handful of employees, traders and technical people, gathered around a Sun workstation.  They were talking about Bermudan swaptions.

I assumed that this was some kind of hazing ritual in which they invented gobbledegook to confuse the new guy, but, no, they assured me, this was a real thing:   If, they explained, an American option could be exercised on any day from the day it was struck until it expired and a European option could only be exercised on the final day, its expiration, then a Bermudan option was halfway in-between, just like Bermuda was between America and Europe:  It could only be exercised on specific dates.  And a swaption was short for a swap option, or an option on a swap.  And a Bermudan swaption was a common way of allowing a firm entering a swap to get out of it on any given payment date.[ct]

As the markets seesawed, ICB found itself losing on both sides of the market, with the hedges going the wrong way, and hedging often creating additional losses.   Some days our unit lost $50mm.[da]

Being a quant was fun.  I learned that when a quant told me that a model was too complicated to explain, it usually meant that he was hiding something, some weakness or limitation.  I became good at sniffing those out and explaining to the traders how to evaluate the quant work.  And good at getting the quants to focus on making their models work for real problems, rather than continuing to polish them for theoretical beauty.

During this time I developed a small formula parser for representing financial product payouts to be evaluated using pricing models.  And a small XML-based language for describing the products.  These proved to be helpful in the ensuing few years.[dc]

ICB’s losses spurred by the Asian contagion continued, and other problems surfaced.  We couldn’t make money in the vanilla swap flow business.  Some of the exotic products had unexpected losses during hedging.  Volatile commodity markets were tricky to navigate.   The losses continued.[dd]

I was skeptical.  My experience with the Internet by then was that no one had any idea what would fly.  And that the most successful entrants were the ones that released stuff quickly and often, changing it based on customer feedback.

I proposed that instead of building the space shuttle, we start with a little three month project, to display near real-time streaming prices on a continuously updating web page, with a “click to trade” button that would display a trader’s phone number to call when a customer wanted to trade.  I figured that this would get us a product out to market quickly, let us start to engage with clients, and would be a cheap and cool-looking way to advertise our products.  Then over the coming year or two we could gradually add new functionality as client demand told us.

We hired a huge high tech team from a market data vendor to start designing and building a fully custom solution based on real time messaging. Our “tactical” solution cost quite some millions and took nearly a year to build and deploy.  The “Space Shuttle” solution based on real-time messaging - well, that tanked, and never delivered anything.   A year later, after a huge amount of stress and conflict, we had a nearly working  tactical solution, and we’d spent $50 million getting there.  I fought battle after battle to keep costs under control, only to regularly be overruled by Antonio and Jim.   On weekends I would go to my country place in the Poconos and work on my water garden, working for hours on digging out dirt and installing retaining walls.  The physical effort, banging a pick into the ground to loosen the rocks, and shoveling away the dirt, was my main stress outlet.

TradeWiz was eventually fully launched.   By the end of its first full year of operation it had successfully executed several dozen client trades, each worth maybe up to a hundred dollars in revenue.  The revenue generated by the system in the year wasn’t enough to pay for a month of its datacenter rental, much less to justify the millions spent developing it.  It turned out that most TradeWiz users preferred to look at the real-time streaming prices and call their trader, essentially as I originally proposed.[dh]  It was shut down a couple of years later, after I had left CabotLodge.   When I heard that I felt a well-justified schadenfreude[di].[dj]
