Anya, who’d also drawn Colleen, was also gaga about drawing her, and asked if she could join us when Colleen posed at my place.  This seemed like a reasonable idea, so we would have a chaperone, to make things feel a bit safer.    Following all of this, I double checked with Mary that she was ok with all of this.  (I’d already told her of my dream and got her approval.)  Mary said she was fine with Colleen posing, but we’d need to find a way to keep the kids away during the session.)  I agreed with Colleen that we’d do a few sessions until the drawings were satisfactory.

After this, Anya struck up a friendship with Colleen, quickly finding out about her:  Colleen was an actress who had moved recently from BC to Toronto.  Anya and I watched the independent film that she had a supporting role in.   And even better, Colleen would soon be playing on stage in a major role in a fairly large theatre in North York, with a well-regarded community theatre company.   Anya and I decided to go together.

The night of the performance Anya and I drove up to the theatre and parked.  A few minutes early, we decided to chat.  As we waited in the car, my phone rang.  It was Juliet, panicky, calling from home.  She said something indecipherable but scary, something about hurting herself.  The call dropped.  Frantic, I called back, but Juliet didn’t answer.    But I received a text from her, saying that she had decided to end it, and had cut her wrist.

Terrified, I called Mary.  She told me that she had been at the museum with Brad, and had left Juliet at home, and that she was sure Juliet would be fine, that she was acting out.  Mary would be home in twenty minutes or half an hour and would be able to see what was going on.

This calm dismissal of what seemed to be a life-threatening situation just didn’t compute for me.  I decided that I needed to act.   I called 911 and spoke to the emergency operator, who promised to send police with a mental health response unit.  They would be there in a few minutes, she said. Juliet would be safe.

I held my head in my hands for a moment, unsure what to do, feeling quite at a loss.  Then after a few seconds I told Anya I was going home.  I waited only long enough for her to hop out of the car, so at least she could see Colleen perform, and headed back home as quick as I could go.

The ride back was surreal:   I headed along highways at breakneck speeds, weaving through traffic in ways I’d seen assholes do, but never done myself.  I came down the winding Don Valley Parkway often exceeding 150 km/h, feeling like a Formula 1 race car driver as I weaved around the other cars, taking curves inside or outside to get the best line, drifting across lines and through the shoulder.  I didn’t care if I was stopped for speeding - I had something more important to do.

I was home in no time - under 15 minutes, much less than the usual trip time for the 24 kilometer trip. [ep]  When I arrived at home, there was a collection of emergency vehicles gathered around our home, flashers spinning.   I raced inside, my heart in my mouth.

There were several uniformed cops standing around talking among themselves.  Juliet was sitting on a sofa, talking with a female police officer, looking pale but otherwise healthy.  There was no sign of blood.   A cop pulled me aside to make a statement:  Where had I been, what did Juliet tell me, and so on.   Eventually the lady cop came over to me.

Juliet, she said, had apparently smashed one of my art prints, a Victorian lady standing near the spire of St. Martin in the Fields in London, painted by James Tissot, [eq]and had used the shards of the glazing to score her wrists.  But the glazing wasn’t glass, just acrylic, and so it hadn’t cut her, just scratched her.   She was scratched badly, multiple times, but there was no more than a droplet or two of blood.  She said this wasn’t uncommon: teenagers would often attempt to hurt themselves as a cry for attention, but without actually wanting to kill themselves.

The next week everything was disrupted:   Shortly after the suicide attempt, Juliet checked herself into the psychiatric ward at Toronto East General Hospital.  With heavy hearts we accompanied her there, swallowing at seeing the locked ward, protected behind a locked foyer.  We learned that Juliet wouldn’t be free to come and go, and would be expected to stay for a several day period to adjust.

The next day we got an anguished call:  Juliet.  

“Hi mom, hi dad…   This place is terrible.  I can’t sleep.   Kids were moving around all night.  and making noise.  I’m so unhappy!  Please come and take me home!”   Her grief and anguish was palpable.  

I felt a lump in my throat.   We promised to talk to the ward supervisors.

Hours later, after several tense calls with the administrators, we convinced them to spring Juliet from confinement the next morning, if she still wanted out.   We reasoned that since she had been voluntarily committed, and since she no longer wanted to be there, they ought to let her out, even if they normally had a policy to keep patients in for 3 or 4 days.  They had to think about that for a long time, and consult supervisors, but eventually agreed.

At the appointed hour Mary and I arrived at the visitor lobby for the TEGH psych ward. We were told to wait.   We waited, and waited.  Eventually, Juliet shambled outward, lugging her overnight bag, looking forlorn and lost.

I rushed towards her, towards the locked door, arms outstretched, a pang of relief suffusing my chest. Once the door was unlocked and she spied me, she also rushed towards me, a huge smile on her face.  We hugged.    I had a vision of that old movie trope where a man and a woman run across a field towards each in slow motion, eventually embracing in a tight hug.  Mary stood back, with a small smile on her face, then slowly walked over to embrace her daughter.   Then that movie glow faded, and the grim, grey surroundings of the pysch ward reappeared, and we hurried out of that horrible place.

The next few weeks continued unsettled.   I didn’t want to leave Juliet alone, so the next Saturday I took her with me to my art class at my traditional atelier.   A lovely woman with a daughter a little older than Juliet took my daughter under her wing for the day.  I saw the two of them happily talking away and working on art.  My relief and gratitude were immense.  The lady told me that she’d been through a lot with her daughter, so she had some idea of what to do and say.

When I next saw Anya, she was curious about Juliet, and asked me to tell about everything that had happened.  She also told me about the show that Colleen was in, the one that I’d missed:   “It was amazing,” she said.  “If you’d been there, you’d have completely fallen in love with Colleen,” she gushed.  It seemed clear that Anya was also rather taken with the young actress/model.

A few weeks passed before our first drawing session with Colleen.  We found a time when Mary and the kids would be out for a weekend afternoon.   The three of us gathered in the music room of my house, near Mary’s grand piano.   We chatted.   Colleen told us something about her upbringing.  She was kind and caring, if slightly guarded.   She was curious about me and about my children, and asked a lot of questions about Juliet.  

I found myself really enjoying talking with Colleen, her liveliness and warmth and gentle demeanor.    And Anya was beside herself with curiosity about her new friend.    We did some initial sketches that day, but didn’t really make a lot of progress, other than getting to know each other, and feeling a little more relaxed.

Afterward, I found myself in a daze:  I clearly had a crush on Colleen, a huge crush.  I couldn’t believe that this beautiful young woman would be so curious about me.   I loved her questions, her kind and compassionate suggestions.  And the stories she told of her family life, of how she had cared for her disabled brother for years.   She was quiet and thoughtful and curious and warm, not at all how I imagined beautiful women.

At the same time, even though I was having an affair with Anya, so had broken my marriage vows, and even though I could feel a nice, warm vibe from Colleen, I couldn’t really see myself propositioning Colleen.  The age gap was too much, and she clearly was interested in younger men.  I was more of a curiosity, an interesting middle aged man, than a true attraction. My crush was clearly going to be unrequited in the romantic sense, but I could still look forward to seeing her again.

And I could flirt with her … when we had moved from the Poconos to Canada, we had brought our hot tub with us, and I flirted with Anya and Colleen that after our next drawing session we could all jump into the hot tub together, bathing suits not required.   I fantasized about that possibility, but the fantasies went no further than having a fun, naked spin in the hot tub with my sexy mistress and a beautiful young woman who seemed to like us both.   I tried to push my fantasies further, but they wouldn’t go:  Even in my imagination, Colleen was off limits.  I might have a crush on her, but couldn’t imagine having sex with her.  In any event, Colleen playfully flirted back that she’d have to think about the offer, that it might get too hot in the hot tub…  maybe another time.

Eventually a day came when Colleen could pose with the piano again.   It was an evening that the kids were going out to a graduation dance.  They would be out until well after 9 pm when the dance ended, so no need to tell them.  Mary and I arranged that I would do the drawing with Colleen while Mary visited friends.  Anya wasn’t available, so we were unchaperoned last night, but the previous get togethers had relaxed us both, and everything seemed fine.

To keep the spirit of relaxation, I made up a cheese board for us and opened a bottle of wine, of a type that I had acquired that I thought would be to Colleen’s taste.   I put some music on the stereo.  Colleen and I talked, and then she posed for me, and I drew.  My drawings were disappointing:  the figures were ok, but the faces were heavy, crude, ugly.  We tried different poses, to see what would give the best effect.  We talked.  We paused, and had cheese and crackers, and drank wine, which she enjoyed greatly. We talked of family and values. The mood was quiet and relaxed, connective.

All of a sudden, there was a clatter from the front door downstairs.   Startled, I checked the time:  it was shortly after 8, well before the kids were supposed to arrive.   I called[er], “Who’s there?”

Juliet answered, in high spirits, “It’s us, Dad.  The dance ended early.  We had a great time!”

Looking around at the area around me, and imagining how the kids might see it, I was horrified:   I was drinking a glass of wine with a beautiful, young, naked woman, who was sitting at their mother’s piano, also holding a glass of wine.  The wine bottle was nearly empty.  There was the remains of a cheese board on a counter.  Soft music was playing on the stereo.  Mary was out, visiting a friend.  It was clearly, it seemed, a date, not a drawing encounter.  And it would appear to be behind Mary’s back.  And I realized that Juliet would immediately come bounding up the stairs to the open concept second floor, on the way to her bedroom, and see Colleen, not so many years older than she, in the altogether.  With her father.  I felt like I had been caught in bed with a woman other than my wife.  Like I was cheating.

I yelled downstairs, my voice quivering, “Don’t come up!  I’m finishing something and need a few minutes!”

The kids went, “Whaaat!!??”  Colleen looked surprised and bemused, but said nothing.   Downstairs, Brad and Juliet chattered about what was going on, and then about the party they’d been at.

I told Colleen that we’d need to stop now, and clean up, and that she’d need to dress.    She immediately understood, and quickly donned her clothing, while I tried to clear the cheese board and put everything around the piano in position.

A couple of minutes later the two of us headed downstairs, with me looking guilt-ridden and embarrassed.  Colleen just smiled hello to Juliet and hi to Brad, and headed out the door after giving me a hug.

I turned to face them. They gaped at me, clearly wondering who this beautiful young woman was who had so warmly hugged their father and had headed out the door.  “What was going on?  Why couldn’t we go upstairs?”  Their questions were reasonable, but I was tentative and evasive in my answers[es].

“Nothing was going on,” I said, defensively, looking away.  “We were just drawing.”   Juliet looked at me with disbelief and reproach.   She headed upstairs to her room.  On the way through the second floor she passed the remains of our wine & cheese, which cowered forlornly, discarded so unceremoniously a few minutes ago.  Brad silently ducked away to his basement lair.   A few minutes later I headed to the second floor and finished clearing away our snack.  The house was silent.

***

“Phew!” said Dan.  “Uncomfortable!! …. Betcha wish you could have had a do-over on that one!”

I smiled a crooked, sad smile back at Dan.

“Yeah.”

***

The next day, I tried to talk to Juliet, to explain.  She avoided me, never giving me an opportunity, mostly hiding in her room.  We didn’t talk.  Nor the following day.

By the following evening, finding her resting in the living room in the evening after school, I approached her.   “About the other night ….” I started, unsure of who to start.

Juliet interrupted, abrupt. “It’s okay, Dad.  It’s fine.  I get it.  I understand.  I don’t want to talk about it.”  She got up and headed to her room.

I was left speechless, watching my daughter retreat.

I’d explained to Mary what had happened.  She hadn’t said much, but had made a face or two and some disapproving noises. It was clear that she wasn’t impressed.  And it was clear that she wasn’t going to try to explain or mediate or connect.  I was on my own.

Life continued in our home, somewhat out of balance.  Juliet barely spoke with me.  Mary was reserved, saying enough to manage the basic necessities of life, but otherwise apparently uninterested in pursuing discussions about Colleen.   I kept busy, with financial industry conference calls and art classes and spending time out evenings with Anya.

Some days later, one evening at bedtime, Mary started a conversation with me.[et]

“Why are you so mean with the kids?” she started.

Startled, I said, “What?”

She told me that I was always stomping and storming around and yelling.   And that she wanted me to change.  There was a strongly implied “or else.”   It wasn’t clear what the “or else” would be.

I asked her for an example of when I was mean to the kids.

She talked about how I would yell at Juliet in the morning, and stomp around.

She was talking about the days that I would get up before 6 am to make breakfast for Juliet, who had to be at school before 7 for the swim team practices she had unexpectedly signed up for. On those days, I would head up two stories to her room before I started making breakfast, and call at first quietly and then louder from outside her door until she woke up and acknowledged me.  (She would set her alarm, but almost invariably either mess it up[eu] or sleep through it.)   Once she was awak[ev]e, I would head back downstairs and start breakfast.   If there was no sign of movement after another ten minutes or so, I would head upstairs again, this time calling up from the second floor, a little louder.  Then back to the kitchen to keep the eggs from burning. Sometimes I’d have to do this three or more times.  Each time I would go up the stairs faster and need to yell louder to keep everything on schedule.  Because each day we’d have to fly like the wind to get to the school in time for Juliet to make the deadline for practice.

I pondered how this morning routine would sound to Mary.  “Well,” I said, “it’s true that I have to run up the stairs and that I need to yell, but it’s not because I’m angry or mean.   I mean, Juliet asked me to help her get to swim team on time, and wants me to wake her up.”   Mary didn’t look particularly satisfied, commenting that I seemed so angry when I did it, but agreed that this was something that Juliet had asked for.

I asked Mary for another example of how mean I was to the kids.   She brought up the room cleaning incident, talking about how I was mean not to help Juliet clean her room.   This one caught me hard.  I knew I hadn’t handled it very well.  But I wasn’t sure that I’d been mean… but maybe I was?

I had an appointment with my therapist the next day (I’d been seeing her for a few months, at the insistence of both Mary and Anya).  I told her about Mary’s criticism, and asked if she thought I’d been mean during the room-cleaning incident.    We talked it through, again, and she said, “Well, maybe you didn’t handle that as well as you could have, and maybe that could be seen as mean.  But you had some good reasons for what you did, and it doesn’t seem that bad to me.  I’ve seen lots of parents do much meaner things, for no good reason.”

Then she said, “Ask Mary to give you another example of when you were mean to the kids.”

So that night I said to Mary, “OK, I don’t think that incident was so bad, but maybe it was.  I agree that I might have been mean that time.  But can you give another example of when I was mean to the kids?”

Mary paused and thought.  And thought.  And then she said, “Well, your demeanor is mean a lot of the time, but I can’t give you a specific example.”

And I parroted what my therapist suggested. “Well, if you can’t give me an example, it’s hard for me to know what to do to change.   Can you please let me know the next time you think I’m being mean to them?”  Mary looked unimpressed, but reluctantly assented.

A few days later Mary and I went out of the house for a tea to talk about the issues between us, the first of many such get togethers at that coffee house.   It was a difficult, unresolved conversation.  Mary continued to push the theme that I needed to be different.  I told Mary that I wasn’t angry with her, but that I had stopped guessing what she wanted.  If she wanted me to do something, she would need to be specific.  To give specific examples, and make specific requests.  That I wasn’t just doing things anymore because I thought she might like it.   At the end of the conversation I felt better that I felt that had made my position clear, and had stood up for myself.   I’m not sure what Mary thought of the conversation, but I got an inkling in the coming days.

The following Friday night Mary and Juliet performed at the class performance of the introductory belly dancing class they had been attending.  It was a little ironic for me, because I had dragged the whole family to one or two belly dancing events a year or two before - and I had been teased mercilessly for it, told how inappropriate it was, that I was being lewd.  But apparently something had stuck with the ladies in the family.

The performance that night had a wide range of performers, from the nervous and shy beginners, to several excellent and skilled solo performers.   It ended up being a good time, the first time that the family had been together and had a good time for a long time.

That night, as we were in bed getting ready for sleep, Mary said that she had something to tell me.

“I’ve tried to help you but you refuse.  I want you to be better but there’s nothing I can do.  You don’t listen to me when I try to help. You continue to be mean to the kids, and I need to protect them.  I want you to leave.  For the good of the kids.  As soon as possible.”

She lay down and rolled over, facing away from me, silent.   I sat up in bed, stunned.  My stomach churned.  

I stayed there for ten minutes or so, thoughts awhirl, until I realized that I couldn’t sleep in that bed, even stay in that bed, after what Mary had said.    I got out of bed and headed up to my office, which had a sofa, and found some blankets, and rolled up in it.   I spent hours lying awake, tossing and turning, wondering what to do, worrying about what was next.   Wondering about how and when we would tell the kids.  Eventually, I slept.

***

The next morning, a Saturday, I emerged groggily from my office to talk to Mary.

“How are we going to tell the kids?”  I asked, rubbing the sleep from my eyes.

“I already told them,” she responded, tartly.  “I got them up early to talk to them.  I told them that I asked you to leave to protect them, because you weren’t good for them right now.  I said that you loved them and had tried your best to be a good father, but …”   The rest that she said faded into a blur, as a roaring filled my ears.

I was stunned.   This didn’t seem like how you tell the kids about something like this.  Shouldn’t I have been part of the conversation? Shouldn’t the explanation of why I was leaving be left vague?   Did I really need to be thrown under the bus in front of the kids?

The day proceeded awkwardly.    There were some stilted conversations with the kids.  Juliet  said,  “Mom told us that you are moving out.   I know you did your best, Dad.  I hope things get better for you.”  Then she bustled off; neither of us knew what else to say.

I wrote to Anya about what happened, telling her how stunned I felt.  Then I realized that this meant that the sessions with Colleen and the piano couldn’t happen anymore.  I wrote to my lovely model and new friend, briefly explaining the situation, and hoping that we could somehow continue to work together.  I expressed my sadness, and commented in closing that this was made harder for me because I had developed complicated feelings towards her in our time working together, and I was afraid of losing that closeness.

Mary and I had previously made plans to go to the Art Gallery of Ontario that afternoon with the kids to see a special exhibition.   With the news, Mary begged off going, saying that it didn’t feel right to her, and Brad decided to stay with her.  Juliet, I think feeling sorry for her dad, said she would come with me.

We headed off to the exhibit.   As we sauntered around, an email came in from Colleen.   She briefly expressed sadness about my marriage falling apart, but very quickly came to the point:  She was highly disturbed by my comments about my “complicated feelings” towards her, and said that knowing about these, she wasn’t prepared to continue to pose for me…. That it was too much for her.

As I read her words, I understood where she was coming from… clearly she didn’t want to pose for a middle aged man in the middle of a breakup with his wife who had apparently confessed his love and desire for her.   (Which isn’t exactly what I was trying to say, but I could see it could be read that way, and maybe it wasn’t too far from the truth.)   But this rejection - for it felt like a rejection - was almost too much to bear.  The only person in my life who was consistently kind and sweet to me, someone whom I’d come to enjoy and admire very much, had just in effect told me I wasn’t safe to be around.

I was suddenly stunned with pain and grief.   The numbness that I’d been feeling since Mary’s announcement instantly shifted into pain. The tears welled up in my eyes, and a huge lump formed in my throat.   I wavered on my feet.  Fortunately Juliet was a few feet away when I read this, and I eased further away from her, and around a corner.    I wept quietly for a few minutes, acutely aware of my losses.  Of how life was changing, and of how people close to me were rejecting me.   I wondered if I deserved it.  I thought that perhaps I did.  Perhaps I wasn’t a good person, after all.  Perhaps I was a truly awful person who deserved to be shunned.

The day continued in a blur.  I gathered myself together, choking down the tears and putting on a grim, blank face for Juliet. I answered Colleen that I understood, and that I hoped she could continue a friendship with Anya.  I wrote to Anya about the debacle, and made plans to see her the next day.   That night, Mary went to our regular book club.  I stayed home, feeling miserable.

Meeting Anya, the next day at a public square, wasn’t much better.  She commiserated with me about what had happened with Mary, and wondered what I would do next.  But she quickly turned to the email exchange with Colleen, and excoriated me for what I had written to her.  Couldn’t I see how threatening what I had written would be for Colleen?  Couldn’t I see that I was implicating her in my breakup, and how unsettling that would be for her?  Couldn’t I see I had pushed Colleen away, that it was all my fault? Why couldn’t I be more aware of the impact of my actions on others, Anya as well as Colleen?  And how dare I encourage Colleen to stay friends with her?  As she spoke I understood that it was her way of dealing with the grief of what happened, but that didn’t make it all feel better.  I just felt like I had let everyone down in my life.  Tears pricked at my eyes, but I held them back, as best as I could.

Chapter 15: More Relating

Back at the farmhouse, our gang took stock.  Monica went to put on dry socks and to warm herself by the fire.  Cassandra and Jack started working on dinner.

Dan reflected on my story.  “Gutting.  That’s absolutely gutting.  How did you handle it?”

***

After the crushing blows of mid-June 2013, things continued to get worse.  A few days after all this, Mary encouraged the whole family to go and get ice cream together, as a stab at normalcy.  We walked some blocks over to a local gelateria, picked out our sugary delights, and then ambled awkwardly back home.  Conversation was desultory and muted.

The next day Mary and I visited a marriage counselor I had found who I hoped could help us with co-parenting.  My hopes of reconciling with Mary were dim at best.  I told the counselor that the engagement was not about reconciling, but only about establishing a peaceful co-parenting arrangement, not realizing that her focus was only on saving marriages - that I had chosen the wrong kind of advisor.

Mary launched in.   “Brian is a threat to the kids, and I need him out of the home now.  The kids keep telling me how mean he is to him, and how they don’t want him around.  He needs to learn to be kinder.”

I was stunned, blindsided.  Why was this the first I heard about the kids’ complaints about me?  Why did Mary encourage us all to go out together for ice cream the previous day if I was a threat to the kids?   Why did it seem that this was a stab in the back, carefully chosen to cause the most damage, delivered with the greatest possible surprise?

I stuttered and stammered to the counselor that I didn’t know what to say, that this was all news to me.  The counselor pursed her lips and looked down her nose at me, clearly disappointed that I wasn’t aware of what my children thought, that I was out so of touch.   This incident was the first of a series of incidents - with lawyers and psychiatrists and advisors - where Mary calmly  pulled out the most damning possible information about me, generally unsubstantiated, making me look as bad as possible in front of an authority.  But in direct conversation with me she had nothing to say.  I felt like a clumsy bull stabbed by an agile toreador.

The counseling session continued, and we set out our goals for counseling.  I reiterated that I wasn’t there for reconciliation, just to figure out how to co-parent.  The counselor suggested that we meet with her individually in the coming week.

I met with the counselor and asked her whether any information I shared was confidential.  In other words, if I told her something, could I trust that she would not pass this information onto Mary?  She assured me that everything I said was confidential, that I could trust that she wouldn’t pass it on, that I was at liberty to tell her anything.   So I told her the situation.   I told her that I was having an affair, and that I didn’t want the marriage to reconcile, just to figure out how to co-parent.   The marriage counselor stopped me:  “You need to tell her immediately about the affair if you want me to continue as your marriage counselor.  Without that, there’s no way to reconcile.”

“Weren’t you listening?” I asked.  “I don’t want to reconcile.  I’m here about co-parenting.  I’m not interested in reconciling”   She was adamant, refusing to consider my offer to tell Mary about the affair within a set number of sessions.  She would refuse to continue unless I came clean immediately about the affair.

I talked about it with Anya.  She refused to let me share that I was having an affair, because she was afraid that Mary would come after her.  And I was terrified of losing Anya, after losing Mary, and possibly the kids, and definitely Colleen, and who knew who else, though by this time it looked like I had also lost most of my friends in the book club.

So I reluctantly canceled any further sessions with the counselor, having to tell Mary that based on my one-on-one conversation with her, I had lost trust in the counselor’s ability to help us.  

As this happened, we continued to speak with various therapists and advisors for the children about how we were managing their challenges and the separation.   It seemed like each time Mary would pull out a surprising, damning statement about me.   Afterwards, I would check in with her one on one to see if there was anything more, and she would say no, she’d shared it all.  Then the process would repeat.

 Each time something like that happened, I felt an agonizing tearing, like the bonds of commitment between me and Mary were ripped, painfully.  I found my respect and remaining love for Mary taking immense hits.  I realized that she was fighting in ways that I found unfair, manipulative, nasty.   My image of her took a nosedive.  I suddenly realized that she wasn’t a friend attempting to make the best of a difficult situation.  Instead, she felt like a deceitful and manipulative enemy, who would withhold damaging information until the most damaging possible moment, in front of professionals she believed would be supportive.

Meanwhile, Mary and I talked every few days about what was going on between us.  I demanded that she take responsibility for part of the breakdown - to acknowledge that it takes two to make a relationship.   Eventually one time, through gritted teeth, and obviously under duress, she acknowledged that her behaviour had some impact on the relationship.  That she had some responsibility for its outcome. Then I realized that I needed to pack for my trip and to head to the airport, and that I had better get busy.

The Greece trip was tough.   All the way there I grieved what had happened with Cassandra, and wondered what to do about Sal.   At a layover I wrote Cassandra, telling her that I was thinking about giving up on polyamory, that I missed her terribly.  There was no word from Sal.

Brittany and I fought again several times on that trip.   At Naxos, where Cassandra had lived for six months early in her career, I felt bereft, grieving that I was with Brittany rather than her.  Disgusted with me, Brittany took off on her own, telling me she needed some space while I got out of my funk.  It felt ugly.  I wrote Cassandra a long, heartfelt email about how sad I was.  She wrote back that she’d found another partner, and that he was the one, and that her heart swelled with joy.  And to forget her.

Somehow I made it through the trip.  Brittany and I slogged through various Greek Islands, eating rich vegetarian food and seeing the sights.   It was hard to be sad in those surroundings, so eventually I settled into a quiet, contemplative frame of mind.

On my return, I told Brittany I needed space.  I wasn’t breaking up with her, but I needed some alone time.

Chapter 22: Reset

Jack said,  “That sure was a tough time for you, wasn’t it, buddy?”

Heartbreak.

On my return from Greece, all I felt was heartbreak.  Cassandra, the woman I loved, and whom this latest fling had deepened my feelings toward, despite all the pain, had dumped me.  Sal, with whom I’d had a sparkly, sexy connection, had disappeared, blaming me.  Brittany, with whom I had traveled in Greece, was an enjoyable partner in crime at times, but was not a lot of fun to be around a lot of the time, and wasn’t particularly curious about me, except as to how I would be as a boyfriend.  But perhaps most importantly, my theory about polyamory was in tatters:  I thought that having multiple relationships would insulate me from the shock if one ever ended.  Instead, I found that the multiple relationships would increase the complexity and amplify the shock of a breakup.   The problem was that the common denominator was me.[fd]

I decided that I needed some perspective, ideally from beyond my inner circle of friends, who had been part of this journey.  Normally I would turn to Jack and Amber and Maya for this kind of support, but I’d been involved with all of them, and I wanted to hear from someone who wouldn’t have an axe to grind.   So I reached out to Kate for some perspective and perhaps even some psychotherapy, and to Christina for a few days’ respite in the near North, where I hoped to enjoy canoeing on the open water.

When those attempts at connection failed, the grief and self-judgment multiplied:  no one, I thought, cared for me enough to deal with my shit.  I had a pattern of pushing away or scaring off everyone that I cared for.  It was me, it was inherent in me, and I didn’t deserve friendships or relationships.   And I couldn’t trust that people I might turn to would actually be there in a pinch.  And that I brought it on myself: I took on risks heedlessly. I was selfish and immature.  I couldn’t anticipate the probable results of the crazy decisions I made.  I was oversexed and lacking in judgment.

After wallowing around in self-pity and self-judgment for a few days, I decided that if I couldn’t have friendships, I could at least seek professional help.  I turned to a psychotherapist, recommended by a friend of Brittany’s.  As I prepared to go in to see her, I got an email from Cassandra:   “Let’s go to the Leslie Spit at dawn for a release ceremony.  We can smash dishes to represent the end of our love.”

I imagined the scene:  The two of us in the chilly, dewy morning air as the sun rose across the lake, both of us angry and yelling and smashing plates and bowls.  Then the anger receding, and the two of us passionately fucking on the sharp rocks, as early morning joggers passed nearby.

Meeting her did not seem like a particularly wise idea.

The therapist looked concerned about the depth of my pain and grief, but listened and listened and didn’t say a lot other than that it did sound like I had done some work, and given that over the coming sessions she was confident we could deal with it.

I went home and sat on my deck and listened to the wind in the trees, and sketched, and calmed down a little.  And then I started journaling.   I wrote about what had happened, what were the events, what feelings had come up.  I wrote about my beliefs, my self-judgments.   I wrote down all the brutal things that I was telling myself:  That I was impulsive and selfish and oversexed and careless and heedless. That I couldn’t anticipate the consequences of my actions, and I deserved everything that happened to me.

Then I looked at all of these beliefs and self-judgments.   And I thought about them.  And I realized that it wasn’t true that I hadn't anticipated trouble.  I had been telling people that I didn’t expect the configuration with four active simultaneous relationships would last.  I knew that there was a possibility if not a probability of conflict, of breakups.   It wasn’t that I had proceeded impulsively, without considering the possible outcomes.  It was that I had considered the possible outcomes and proceeded anyway.  I had prioritized growth and experience over stability and safety.  I had seen the risk and taken it anyway.  And I had worked as hard as I could to satisfy my various partners’ desires.  It had been an impossible situation, and I had done my best, and if I looked back at all of the individual decisions I had made, I didn’t feel ashamed or embarrassed about much.  I had behaved in a way I considered met my own integrity, and it hadn’t worked out as I hoped and expected.   It was sad, but nothing to be ashamed about.

I started seeing another way to look at what had happened:  I hadn’t been impulsive and heedless, but rather courageous and daring and considerate to my partners at each step of the way.  When I had pained others, it wasn’t because I didn’t care, but because the situations had been such that there was no other way.  I wasn’t lacking in care or compassion.  It was just that I couldn’t have anticipated all of the drama and conflict that would happen, and I didn’t have the experience to know how it would affect me.

I started to see myself as an unlikely hero, a brave man, an adventurer, who had run into a setback on a tricky journey, rather than a reckless jackass.   Maybe the glass was half full rather than mostly empty.

I also looked into the question of needing to have friends.   I realized that at that moment my best friend was myself.  I could give myself all the time I needed, and I could listen to what I felt and what I needed by meditating and journaling.   And I could occasionally get together with a friend - Jack or Amber or Maya, for instance - to talk about what was going on for me, and where I was in my thinking.   I realized that for my whole adult life I had assumed that I needed someone to take care of me - my partner, or my friends - but that the only person I could truly rely on was myself.

So life was proceeding intensely, with its usual exciting, messy rhythm, when the pandemic hit.

Chapter 23: Peace at Last?

Amber suggested that we head out to the hot tub to watch the sun set.

Dan asked, “But how did you guys get back together? And why did you buy this farm?”

The news from Wuhan  in January 2020 was alarming:  infections spreading exponentially, brutal lockdowns, old people starving to death, cut off from support.  Could this novel coronavirus spread beyond China?

Then by late February it was reported in Bergamo, in Lombardy, Italy, and it was spreading almost inconceivably fast.  This seemed to be no ordinary flu epidemic; it was terrifying.  The Lombardy hospitals were quickly overloaded, people dying like flies.

I took notice.  Nerd that I am, I wondered what the math said.  I put together a spreadsheet showing the growth rate in infections, and possible rates of hospitalization and death.  I extrapolated it, using techniques I’d used in science and on Wall Street.   What I saw looked like Armageddon:   at the current growth rates, we could easily see several million deaths in the USA and Canada in the next few months; worst case, if it all hit quickly, could be tens of millions of deaths.   I rapidly concluded we needed to do something, right away, to slow things down.

I started talking about it with my friends; I posted about it.  People said I was scare mongering, that there was no way.   Most politicians showed no signs of acting.    In my imagination I saw temporary morgues set up all over the city; overflowing hospital triage centers, with people dying waiting to get into a hospital.  And no one seemed to get it.  People kept flying around.  Business meetings kept happening.  Sporting events went on.  My panic mounted.

Finally, as the news went from dire to apocalyptic, the local politicians began to act:  news began to filter out that we would move to a partial lock down in a few days.  I headed to my local supermarket to stock up:  on non-perishable proteins, like cans of tuna, and on basic staples like rice and beans.  At the supermarket, customers moved around, nearly silent, except for couples speaking in hushed, urgent tones, debating what was needed.   There were stories that there was a run on toilet paper;  ironically, I was restocking that week, and found myself challenged to find some rolls for myself.   There was a strange mood to the place, unusual, but it reminded me of something.  Eventually, it clicked:  The odd silence walking home from work on 9/11.

I tried to rally my friends:  Who were the few people I wanted around me if all went to shit?   I got together with Brittany, and Jack, and Amber, and Maya, and we talked about what to do.   Jack was relaxed.  Maya was unsure.  Brittany said we should head for the hills.   I argued that we needed to support each other.   After everyone else had left, Brittany melted down, talking about heading to Ottawa to support her mom, talking about running and hiding.  

I found myself saying, “No, we don’t have time for this.  I get that you are afraid, but this won’t help.  We need to be clear and decisive.”  It was brutal.  Brittany looked stunned by my vehemence.  It was another barrier between us.  She couldn’t understand how I couldn’t empathize with her fear.  I couldn’ understand how she could allow her fear to overcome her judgment in a moment of crisis.   We’d been having a long series of discussions, long walks and talks, and the impression I got was that we both agreed on the issues between us, but neither of us was interested in budging to walk towards the other.   This latest conflict reminded me of the gap.

I started to wonder who I wanted to be with if the world was coming to an end:  Who could I trust?  Who made me feel good?  Who did I long to be around?  

I called Cassandra.   “Why did you reach out to me?   Are you still seeing that guy?”

She sheepishly admitted that it was over with that guy.  And that she was still thinking about me.

As the world around me started to panic and politicians started instituting lockdowns, I started to relax.   The numbers showed that the trajectory of the virus was slowing remarkably.  The worst case scenario wouldn’t be happening.  Even the dire scenarios, if they happened, would take much longer to unfold than originally expected.  Flattening the curve was working.

I went on a long walk with Brittany and told her that I wanted space in my life to work out what I wanted with Cassandra.  And that, as I had learned to my chagrin, meant that I couldn’t be with her as well. And that I didn’t feel that our relationship was working out to either my or her satisfaction, and it wasn’t clear to me that it ever would.  I also said that because it was the pandemic, I wanted to take time apart to take stock, while we were being told by the authorities to self-isolate.  It was a good time to be apart, while things became clearer.  I didn’t want to say we could never get back together, just that it wasn’t in the cards right now.

Brittany listened to me, and agreed that our relationship was challenging, and understood that I wanted time for reflection, and change.  She got it that Cassandra meant a lot to me.  She argued that Cassandra could never give me the life that I wanted, the freedom and openness, but understood that I needed to figure this out for myself. She talked about how much she appreciated my openness, and I listened, and eventually I went home.

Brittany had seemed to take it very well, to my surprise.  Then the next day she wrote to me.  “What, exactly, does this change mean?  Will we still be able to sleep together?  Will we still go on dates?”

I was surprised by this.  Wasn’t she paying attention in the three or four hours we had talked about it yesterday?  Was I not clear? I wrote her a rather short answer that I thought would be clear.  No, we wouldn’t be going on dates.  No we wouldn’t be having sex.  Yes, I would like to remain friends, if she wanted.

“Do you mean you’re breaking up with me?” she wrote back to me. “You haven’t said what you want.”

I didn’t really have a clear answer.  Eventually, I realized what I was asking for was an indefinite pause to sort things out.  And that this wasn’t very satisfying for either of us.

After a day or two of deliberations, I acknowledged it clearly, to myself as well as to her:  I wanted to break up with her, without anger or malice.

At the same time as all of this, I told Maya that I was planning to get back together with Cassandra.  Cassandra got along well with Maya, and she didn’t have a problem if I continued to see Maya.  But Maya withdrew, recognizing the intense energy between Cassandra and me..

I found myself going out several times a week for long walks with Cassandra, as we talked about the future we wanted together, and wondered when it would be safe to meet inside.  We had Zoom dates in the bath or by the fire, and ramped up our desire to be together.  After some weeks of this frustration, as it became clear that the pandemic would neither be as catastrophic as I had feared, nor be over as soon as we hoped, we decided that it was time to get together.[fi]

For our reunion we decided to bubble up and take a trip:  an MDMA trip to reconnect.[fj]

***

The pandemic went on far longer than anyone expected.  Cassandra and I spent a lot of time together; when we met with friends we would do it outside, for a walk or a chilly snack or drink in a backyard or a front step.

I noticed that some of my friends weren’t being so strict about following the lockdown rules.  Some of them were getting together with other friends of mine for sexy parties.

While I had no moral problem with the sex, I did have a problem with the pandemic violations.  And meanwhile Cassandra and I, while theoretically open to playing with others, weren’t even having dinner with others inside.  I started wondering [fk]if Cassandra had done the old bait and switch with me.

But just before the pandemic hit I started to get to know Monica, a sweet and demure looking woman. She was curious about my experience with polyamory.   As we talked, I realized that this sweet-looking woman was leading a bit of a double life:  She and her partner Dan were avid swingers.

Dan and Monica and Cassandra and I got together on my back deck for wine and cheese, to get to know each other a bit and to share stories.   The snacks led to a conversation that lasted nearly six hours.  By the end I was thinking it might be fun to play with Dan and Monica.  And the pandemic seemed to be letting up a bit…

***

We had a fun few get togethers with Monica and Dan… we gradually moved from parallel play to full on sex.   Then came disaster, from an unexpected front:  bedbugs.[fl]

A while back Cassandra had rented the tiny, newly renovated basement apartment in her house to an obese security guard.  After three years, he moved on.  When Cassandra went to clean the place three days after he left (the pandemic was on the upswing again and we were being careful), she found that the place was crawling.   The guard, in addition to being a quiet tenant, apparently was an ideal host for a large menagerie of small pests.

Cassandra was shocked and terrified, and began emptying and demolishing the apartment, after getting it treated for bedbugs.

But the bugs didn’t disappear after the treatment, nor after a second.  In fact, absent their generous host, they started appearing throughout her house, looking for food.  Cassandra started spreading diatomaceous earth around her house, and putting tape along the stairwells and doorways.  Her home began looking like a war zone.  She coughed, not from Covid but from the white powder filling her lungs.  She panicked.

I did what I could: I held space for her, and listened to her worries.  I took in her cat, which was having problems with the dust in the air at home.  Eventually, Cassandra started living with me, staying for some weeks until the five, or maybe the sixth, bug treatment finally did the trick.

All of this happened in the dead of winter during an intense Covid lockdown.   There was nowhere to go other than my place.  Despite the stress, and Cassandra’s panic, our relationship held firm.  There were one or two days that I became overloaded and withdrew a little, but then I rallied and turned back to her with love.

It was during this period of adversity that it began to dawn on us that we might finally have what it takes to make a go of a very long term relationship, and even living together.

***

Cassandra and I had fantasized about buying a farm together for four or five years.  On our long drives to Chicago and down East in 2017 we spun dreams of a farmstead with chickens and goats and a donkey.  Taylor could take care of the animals sometimes, we thought.  We could move in together in Toronto.   But when I asked Cassandra how she would feel if I brought another partner home to her house (which would become our house) she was shocked:   She could never allow that.  How could I imagine that she would?[fm]

Our farm fantasy disappeared in a puff of smoke.

During the summer of 2020, as the pandemic waxed and waned, we looked at a small farm east of Toronto.  We started to imagine how we might swing it to buy it together… but after some time Cassandra decided it would only work for her if the coming generation, her kids or maybe mine, would join us in the endeavor.  But we quickly realized that none of them were in a position to do so. So we dropped the plan.

The next spring, Cassandra’s son Hank came up with an idea:  Why couldn’t  we join forces and buy a farm together?

After some hemming and hawing, Cassandra and I agreed to join the plan.  Before we knew it, we had signed up to buy a farm with several buildings north of Toronto.[fn]

The next months were intense:  Arranging the financials, the insurance, the legalities.  Preparing the move.  Getting the new place set up.  Installing a new heating system.  (We put in a heat pump, expensive but energy efficient.)[fo]  Sorting out issue after issue with the water, the water pump, and the well. Applying paint.  Filling holes.   Etc., etc., etc.

Then came the big step:  I finished packing my things and moved out of my apartment, and in with Cassandra.  My things were split between her place in the city, and our new farm.

Preparing for the move triggered waves and waves of grief:  Was I giving up my freedom, my independence?  Was I making a mistake?

Eventually the deed was done.   Cohabitation turned out to be a blessing, with a bump or two along the way.

We made friends with the neighbors at the farm.  We reached out to my community again, after considerable distance during the height of the pandemic.   We decorated our places to suit our ideas of art and design and sensuality.

We started to host some sexy events, and to connect with an increasingly broad community.  We played with Monica and Dan.  We had a sexy birthday party for me, at which Cassandra and I did a sexy scene.  We increased our circle of friends and play partners

Finally, over a year after we got our farm, our hot tub arrived.  And we planned a party - this party - to celebrate all that we had done.[fp]

***

We all lounged, naked in the warm water, as the dusk settled into night.  Cassandra’s hand reached over and started playing with my cock, deliciously.  I turned and smiled at her.  She smiled back and stretched both of her arms over her head, briefly exposing her breasts over the surface of the water.  To my surprise, the hand kept playing with my cock - apparently it wasn’t hers.  I looked the other way:  It was Jack, grinning devilishly at me.

“Nice job, buddy!”  he said.  “Thanks for inviting us!”

The others smiled and echoed, “Hear, hear!”
